### Post by geoffp on 2006-11-15
If you think the only reason Linux isn't taking off is inertia, you've got blinders on.  It's not taking off because it's not good enough to take off yet.  Let me define the word "good" as I use it here.

I have a co-worker who tried out Ubuntu recently on his laptop.  He and his wife needed that computer for two things: web browsing, and printing.  They loved everything about Ubuntu -- the setup, the experience, the interface, the philosophy.  Only they don't use it, because their Dell printer won't work with it.  Now they have XP on that laptop.  Oops.

If we can't think broadly enough to embrace a limited and temporary compromise on drivers, Linux will never be widely adopted.  And yes, that's a goal, which I personally have for both altruistic and selfish reasons.

### Post by Steve Max on 2006-11-16
I honestly cannot believe people are considering this idea. I cannot believe some people want "Ubuntu mass adoption", when the final system will not follow the Ubuntu philosophy, or the Linux philosophy, or even the Free software philosophy. Is the current Ubuntu userbase so blind that they want to turn it into Windows just to make Windows users happy?

People switch to Linux because it's better than Windows. Safer, more stable, faster, more customizable... A binary blob by default will by definition remove two of those advantages (safer and more stable), and possibly others (it's not always faster, and removes one or more aspects of customization). Also, anyone who has ever used Beryl knows that it is far from being ready for prime time.

What about older computers? A 300 MHz K6-2 with a Riva TNT2 accepts the nvidia driver without problems (no more than the usual), so it would be installed by default. Beryl would also be installed by default. A machine like this runs KDE (without all eye candy) very well, but it would be dead with Beryl. I've installed Linux on many old machines that cannot take a new version of Windows; do you really want those out of your picture? Remember that there are more machines built from 1990 to 1005 than those built in 2006.

There are no PPC binary drivers. So, the default Ubuntu PPC will be completely different than the default Ubuntu x86; and more stable. Is this all Apple's way of selling old Macs? :)

People voting "yes" here are in the wrong place. They don't want "Ubuntu", they want "Windows for free". If they even care to read the [Ubuntu philosophy]("http://www.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/philosophy"), which defines the distribution, they would see that Ubuntu CANNOT include non-free software by default. If you don't like the philosophy, fork it. This is the magic of open source.

### Post by Jimmy_r on 2006-11-16
As I see it: Ubuntu already includes non-"free" stuff on default. Let them include non-"free" 3d drivers. If anyone want to be free of the filthy closed-source things, why are they not using gNewSense already? They surely would have compatible hardware. They would also need a "free" bios and such to really be free though(or as I would like to say: Bound to be "free").

In fact i do not think Nvidia managers will be sleepless because a bunch of geeks do not want their drivers. And to spoil the party: Your choice of drivers do NOT make a difference. Only your choice of hardware does.

Scenario A:
Right now we have two different drivers for Nvidia cards, nvidia-glx and nv.
Lets say Ubuntu have nvidia-glx installed by default. This will NOT kill off the nv driver. In the very unlikely event that nvidia stops supporting their driver on linux, nv will still exist.

This will cause more people to use Ubuntu. It will not, however, cause Nvidia to open up their drivers. Should Ubuntu manage to become as big as windows, they might however be able to make some pressure for opening of hardware specs.


Scenario B:
Lets say Ubuntu has nv installed on default, and prevents the user from installing nvidia-glx. This will not kill off the nvidia-glx driver, but it might, in the long run, make developers improve the nv driver.

This will cause less people to use Ubuntu. It will NOT force Nvidia to open their drivers, or their specs. Ubuntu will stay a niche operating system without any power to change anything. Should developers against all odds finally manage to make the nv driver support all nvidia cards with 3d-acceleration and everything, then ubuntu might start being used more widely. But by that time there could be other, new, operating systems which would render Ubuntu obsolete.


Real scenario:
Ubuntu will do the logical thing:
Educate the user about non-free drivers
Install non-free drivers and make free drivers an option for users who want to change the world :p 
And now the important one:
Tell users their hardware is unsupported, tell them what hardware is supported so they know for their next computer. THIS will make the difference that software preferences on a private machine will not.

The users will (hopefully) find that ubuntu is good, even with unsupported hardware, and will buy an ubuntu-certified computer next time.
If they are many enough (because of the superiority of ubuntu ;) ) they might even make a difference.

---

### Post by misha680 on 2006-11-17
Hi I thought I'd contribute my two cents by way of a little story. I like open source. I like open source a lot. I was using fglrx + Xgl with my mobility radeon 9000, getting great performance, and btw I used to think that Xgl and beryl/compiz were all fluff too until I actually started using them. Yes, I agree that there is a lot of fluff in them, but some things like the rotating cube desktop are really nice, and when you spend a lot of hours staring at your screen having something nice to stare at really makes a difference, at least for me. Another thing is that I have already converted two people in my lab to Ubuntu from Windows, and I think the only reason this happened (at least initially) is because of the 3D effects. People see it and think it is so cool you wouldn't even believe how even random people in coffee shops or the library ask me about this. I think Beryl/Compiz, whether we like it or not, will have a _huge_ impact on adoption of Ubuntu and Linux in general.

But getting back to my story, I am really excited about open source. I was waiting for a while for AIGLX + open source r200 drivers with 3D support, and finally everything was available to me, I installed AIGLX + the latest r200 drivers, feeling good about not using any proprietary drives ... I was already ready to give up my power management with fglrx because, well, I don't need it that much, and I really like open source. But, unfortunately, for my computer and graphics card the r200 drivers just aren't up to snuff... I can use beryl/compiz with these drivers in 16-bit color, but believe me it does look very different (e.g., the "grey" color of update manager dialog begins to have a pinkish tint, and at least to my eye looks _very_ different) or I can use 24-bit with a _lot_ of tweaking and it is very jittery and unusable. So what did I do? As much as I like open source and the ideas behind them, I really wanted a system that works for me, and honestly once you try Beryl/Compiz you'll get hooked, and it's almost impossible to go back to 2D. Suffice it to say I stuck with fglrx (even though ATI dropped support for my gfx card which I am still quite mad at), because it works, and it works fairly well for all their drawbacks. Yes, I understand the security flaws of having such drivers, and I wish open source drivers were available for my video card, but I think that Beryl/Compiz is one of the big things that Ubuntu has going for it for a lot of non-computer-geek people and having this is quite important for its mass adoption. And if the number of people using Ubuntu increase ten times, say, and these people see the messages in Device Manager that they are using binary drivers and start buying recommended open source driver hardware (Intel chipsets for gfx for example), I bet ATI and Nvidia will notice more than if people simply send them complaining emails that they can't use the gfx cards they already bought on their linux distro. So to me it might be a good way to ultimately achieve the same goal of having open source drivers. Anyhow, just my two cents.

Misha

### Post by prizrak on 2006-11-17
> I was already ready to give up my power management with fglrx
This actually another issue with FOSS drivers (only for AMD so far tho), when it comes to mobile computing battery life is very important and GPU throttling is about 15-25 minutes of extra life. At least my tablet running Ubuntu with the nVidia driver (no power management) gets about 2:30-2:45 (rated @ 3:30) out of the battery, Windows with GPU power management gets around 3 hours. If I let the battery go to about 1% then I can get 3:10 or so. This is real functionality that you cannot deny, no eyecandy stuff.

### Post by Miguel on 2006-11-17
> **misha680 said:**
> I was already ready to give up my power management with fglrx because, well, I don't need it that much, and I really like open source.

The beauty of open source is that, although it might not be as "saving" as ATi Powerplay, we r300 users do have some power management features. Just add this line to the device section of /etc/X11/xorg.conf:
```

Option "DynamicClocks"   "on"

```

Just for reference, my current settings are close to the recommended X.org values. I say this because current Ubuntu defaults are *not* the fastest for r300. If you are curious, my X.org conf says:
```

Section "Device"
        Identifier      "ATI Mobility Radeon 9600"
        Driver          "radeon"
        BusID           "PCI:1:0:0"
        Option          "DynamicClocks"         "on"
        Option          "GARTSize"      "64"
        Option          "AccelMethod"   "XAA"
        Option          "AGPMode"       "1"
        Option          "AGPFastWrite"  "1"
        Option          "EnablePageFlip" "1"
        Option          "ColorTiling"   "1"
EndSection

```

I will add that if hibernates perfectly. It used to sleep, but some kind of regression or "user input" made it crash on wake up from sleep.

### Post by Artemis3 on 2006-11-17
No, this practice must cease. What did you use ubuntu in the first place? Propietary drivers (aka blobs) make secure and stable libre systems insecure and unstable. Already nvidia drivers have caused security problems, with many stability issues along the road. IMO, users should go back to vista if they are willing to lose freedom and reliability in ubuntu.

If ever, the installer should warn users with an option to use or not blobs. Not just nvidia, anything like ndiswrapper as well. The most popular distro is also the most influent, if you truly want free software to succeed, you have to get rid of blobs. Blobs are the main cause of problems in support channels already, it is simply impossible to maintain a black box you have no control of.

Also, it is a passing trend, really. The majority of hardware products are open and documented, any device with open drivers works perfectly and flawlessy. When nvidia became popular, the problem rose up, and then their binary drivers came and people thought it was a blessing when it really made a nightmare.

Luckily ATI did not make good drivers, which means their users are actually against them. Their loyalty will not go unrewarded, now that AMD owns ATI, things are changing for the best. I don't know about current chipsets, but they have announced they will include GPUs in their multicore CPUs, and that they are opening the instruction set for the GPUs because thats what made AMD so succesful in the past. In short, AMD believes in open hardware, as opposed to what the previous ATI owners did; and x1000 style gpus can be reprogrammed to do many useful things beyond 3d :)

This leave us with nvidia. Once we start seeing AMD/ATI open, the pressure will build against them. Before they existed, 3dfx was an open 3d maker. Creative labs also tried with the SBlive becoming closed but could not survive pressure from the community (the hardware was bad enough) and their drivers sucked.

Including blobs is already bad enough and in violation of the ubuntu phillosophy: > Every computer user should have the freedom to run, copy, distribute, study, share, change and improve their software for any purpose, without paying licensing fees. Propietary drivers are part of their software which they can't study, share, change or improve. Its no different of using Opera as the default web browser, yet that goes into commercial. Restricted is really nonsensical and should merge with commercial.

The most strong impression a user can get from free software is the "just works". This is only true with open hardware, with closed blobs it is not. It might work today and become broken tomorrow, they will frustrate and go back to vista anyway. The quality has always been high as long as no blobs existed, once they started to permeate the community, that perception is going down so fast as to think there is no difference, and they are right, because there is not. The openbsd stance of zero tolerance to propietary drivers is the right choice. It is better to have less hardware always working 100% than introduce pretty eye candy or wireless things that will break you system.

The community must react against this.

---

### Post by ericesque on 2006-11-20
Dean, I'm on your side of the isssue, but it appears that others are correct in their assessment of the poll results.

The POLL question is: 
Should binary NVidia/ATI drivers be **automatically installed&activated** in Ubuntu?

The question does not mention anything about being default.  Although the word automatically could be construed to mean that it is a default action and the user has no choice, the poll options make it clear that automatically is referring to the user not having to figure it out on their own.

If automatically had meant 'as default' then the third poll option would be irrelevant.  The user can't have a choice if it has already been done by default. However, if automatically is taken to mean that installing the drivers is taken care of by a scripted process, you can first give the user a prompt to ask if they would like the drivers to be installed.

Thus, to say that voters who selected 'give me a choice' are effectively saying No to the poll question, would be incorrect.  This isn't to assume they saying yes to the poll question either.  

I still say it's bad mojo to include them.  Go check out Mint if you really want proprietary everything installed. But hopefully this post will clear the air on that particular issue.

---

### Post by deanlinkous on 2006-11-20
What else COULD automatic installation and activation mean? How else can something be automatic and yet manually chosen? Espcially with the word activated which would mean "put in use".

It doesn't make the third choice irrelevant IMO. Thats right if it is done by "default" they cant have a choice and by making the third selection THAT is exactly what you are arguing is to have a choice in the matter instead of it being automatic. :)

automatic -
largely or wholly involuntary;
acting or done spontaneously or unconsciously 

install -
to set up for use or service

activate - 
to make active

So you honestly think I am the ONLY one that took it to mean by default? AFAIK that is what the spec calls for is it not? From the spec "proprietary drivers will be installed by default," This poll is suppose to relate to the spec is it not?

YES - installed, activated, nothing to ask, nothign to see, nothign to do. Everything setup and running. While informing the user. (what are you informing)

NO - not installed, not activated, and not on the CD either

Users should be given the choice during installation - means that NO they are not treated the same as the YES choice or the NO choice. But it is a direct NO to the actual wording of the poll question since we do not want it to be automatic or activated but simply a radio button in which we choose.

I think the poll question should of been 
"How should proprietary video drivers be handled"
1-on the cd and automatically installed and activated by default
2-not on the cd, left in the non-free repo
3-on the cd and during installation the user given a option of which to use

But please feel free to ignore my ramblings since Ubuntu IMO just turned proprietary (which is fine) but has (and does) claim to be free software (which is not fine).

### Post by kripkenstein on 2006-11-21
Hmm, there seems to be a lot of debate about the intent of the words of the poll. When I wrote it, I had no idea it was that open to interpretation... What I meant was:

1. Automatically install&activate binary drivers: the user installs Ubuntu from the CD. Installation looks just like it does with Dapper or Edgy, from the user's perspective (no questions about video options). But after installation, the binary NVidia or ATI driver is active.

2. No (such drivers can stay in a non-free repo, just like now): this means to keep things exactly as they are now.

3. Users should be given a choice about this during installation: During installation from the CD, the user is asked, "would you like to install&activate a binary driver for your graphics card?" The user can choose to do so, or not to do so.

---

### Post by bob_c_b on 2006-11-25
First, as others have posted before, [Linux != WIndows]("http://linux.oneandoneis2.org/LNW.htm"), nor should it try to. Windows is a grossly inferior product. It skates by on ubiquity and compatibility, but that compatibility is born of monopolistic and (if not illegal) at least unethical business practices. You are not likely to truly attract a lot of users to Linux promising ease of use based on one driver/blob/package; compromising your values on this issue is truly the top of the slippery slope.

The notion that including non-free drivers will somehow cause a huge Linux growth spurt is unrealistic at best. Any potential Linux user who installs Ubuntu based purely on desktop effects and transparency will likely immediately switch back to Windows when the next episode of Half Life won't work or they can't install iTunes. Linux is about learning as much as using, simplify the install and improve the documentation but don't remove the necessity to understand.

A good number of us (long time Linux users) switched to this distro based on Mark's initial claim of sticking to the letter of F/OSS philosophy and seeming very sincere. I know there are some proprietary blobs currently in the installer, but those are mostly born of necessity. Better to simplify the install process for additional proprietary blobs (wireless, video, etc...) than to chip away at the foundation. Fedora does okay with this method and the don't have nearly the quality of community that Ubuntu does.

Throughout these discussions on this topic I see little winks and nudges about "crusaders" and "users who want to change the world" as if that is a bad thing? If it weren't for free software advocates trying to change the world you wouldn't have Ubuntu, so don't be so quick to disregard this philosophy  or declare your pragmatism trumps idealism.

---

### Post by Raven-sb on 2006-11-26

Personally I feel that Easy Ubuntu and Automatix is the right way to go for users who want to use non-free stuff.

### Post by givré on 2006-11-26
```
whats the news on this, has it been approved?
```
If people would like news on development, they should rather not stick on the forum, but on the ubunutu-devel list.

There is actually a long discussion (in fact started due to the opensuse dev reply to mark post), and colin watson, the guy behind ubiuity propose a rather good solution :
```

At present, the accelerated-x specification calls for it to be the
default. I don't especially like the idea, but it isn't my call. Making
it optional seems like something I can easily do to make things a bit
better for those who don't like binary drivers while not inconveniencing
those who do, and it will potentially make it easier to turn the option
off by default in the future.

Question: should the same checkbox govern binary wireless drivers (e.g.)
too, i.e. turn off the restricted component altogether?
```

If you want to follow the discussion, start from here :
[https://lists.ubuntu.com/archives/ubuntu-devel/2006-November/022596.html](https://lists.ubuntu.com/archives/ubuntu-devel/2006-November/022596.html)

### Post by zetetic on 2006-11-28
Now it's official, Ubuntu will be shipping with proprietary hardware drivers soon: [http://www.markshuttleworth.com/](http://www.markshuttleworth.com/)

I think this is a shame and a fraud. Let's see why:

Ubuntu distro says:
Quote:
«Ubuntu is entirely committed to the principles of open source software development; no part of it will ever be proprietary, and we encourage people to use it, improve it and pass it on.»

So, what part of "no part of it will ever be proprietary" is Mr Shuttleworth not understanding?

But the worst is not this lack of honesty.

The worst is: with binary drivers who can assure me (or any Ubuntu user) that my system won't be totally controlled by the hardware manucfacturers that had build the binary drivers??

Who can assure me that none of those drivers is a Trojan Horse that will, for instance, record my passwords and send them to Nvidia, Ati, etc??

It's impossible to know what a binary driver really does... isn't it??

The binary drivers are closed source! So who can assure me those drivers will not take over my system, totally control my OP, record and send my bank passords, my encryption passwords, and so on??

Who can assure me that none of those drivers is a rootkitt???

As far as I know, every driver can be a rootkitt!

Am I misundestanding something here or Ubuntu administrators are loosing their minds?

And if their are not loosing their minds, please explain very well how can they assure any Ubuntu user that his system will not be tottaly compromised!

regards,
zetetic

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-28
[About 85% of Ubuntu users on these forums already use some kind of closed source driver, codec, or software](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=95718), so I think you're really making a big deal over nothing.

One of the great things about Linux is choice. There's no vendor lock-in here. Don't like Ubuntu? [Not free enough for you? Move to Debian, then.](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=307243&highlight=debian) You can take all your config files and use the same software you were using before.

You can't do this with Windows or Mac OS X. Once you've invested in those systems, it's harder to migrate once they decide to throw WGA or something like that on you.

No one says you have to use Ubuntu forever.

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2006-11-29
Ubuntu follows by the ideals of the forces of good (ie: Free software). If they know a proprietary driver has malware, they won't use it.


I'm not going to write much here, but I'll think a few things.
First things first, before I embark on the first "mini-rant" (tm) I have ever written on these forums. I am not directing anything at anyone in particular here, and I hope no-one takes anything personally. If I could avoid the risk of offending someone and still write this, I would. Just the nature of my writing, sadly... it has a pointy edge. Consider it something to munch on, and try not to catch it head on. I can be a tad inflammatory when I write too quickly...


I'd just like to restate something which I wish the wonderful (yes, wonderful; you folks are the reason this all works so well) number of open software zealots would accept.
In an operating system that wants to be for everybody, Hardware support is more important than everything being Open source. Running on everybody's computer is the only way that said operating system could succeed at its goal.
Everything about the operating system is open source; the users of the OS - just like with any good, open OS - have a choice as to whether or not to use all open source software. If you don't want to be using proprietary drivers, use hardware that has open source drivers.
One thing is for sure though: Our good friend Granny Ordinary will be quite happy when her Windows-targeted PC (converted to Linux after she saw *the way*) is *actually working out of the box*, she will be quite pleased with Ubuntu *and with free software in general.* Quite frankly, she will not care that it is not all FREE.

In fact: Once even some of those idealistic Linux users (who want to see it succeed) notice how the end user prefers an OS which _works_ out of the box on their expensive hardware, they, quite frankly, will also be pleased.
The only loss is that we are now relying on some specific developers to support it.
Oh, wait... we weren't relying on *anyone* earlier because *the drivers were hardly there!*
I'm sure if a superior open source driver is developed, Ubuntu will happily use it.


Another thing a bit less ignorant:
The hardware is "closed source", is it not?
These open source drivers could be developed by reverse engineering proprietary hardware.
Remember that CD you used to first install Ubuntu? (The last thing you made with Windows?). I bet that was built with quite a lot of proprietary software. Hmm... this means that the Ubuntu was touched by closed source, and then the one after it was burned with the tainted Ubuntu!
Oh no, the computer isn't perfectly free!
There is always something there which does not quite fit the "open this, open that" ideal. With that in mind, we may as well skip a step. I am sure the computer will not notice.

Maybe do something productive, like developing software to make Linux better?

---

### Post by K.Mandla on 2006-11-29
[gNewSense]("http://www.gnewsense.org/") removes every binary blob and uses specific slices of the Ubuntu framework. 

[quote=gNewSense wiki]gNewSense is derived from Ubuntu, and thus has most of the same functionality. There are a number of differences though.

    * Firmware removed from kernel in main*
    * Restricted removed
    * Ubuntu logos replaced
    * Universe enabled by default
    * emacs, bsdgames, nethack and build-essential part of the default install 

Note our goal is to produce a fully free distribution, not to have as many features as possible.[/quote]
I've tried it. It's really quite good. And if you want to avoid the possibility of a binary hijack, this is as close as you can get to binary-free Ubuntu.

Mark himself suggests using it.

[quote=Mark Shuttleworth]In fact, I worked quite hard to get Gnubuntu (an ISO of Ubuntu without any restricted elements) off the ground - it has effectively now emerged as **gNewSense** and I would encourage you to use that if this is a touchstone issue for you.[/quote]

---

1) Tone down the paranoia. Sure, I can never prove to you that the binary blobs *aren't* trojans or rootkits, but then again, I can never prove to you that there isn't a teapot somewhere out in the Kuiper Belt that shaped the birth and development of the Solar System by mysterious forces. Does that mean it's even remotely likely that there is an omnipotent teapot out in the Kuiper Belt?

Apply some common sense and it would immediately become clear why it's not in a hardware manufacturer's best interests to deliberately infect a customer's computer with malware, and I would refer you to the Sony rootkit debacle for evidence that you don't need access to the source code to detect the presence of malicious software.

2) If you don't like the direction Ubuntu is taking, there is also the GNewSense distribution, which is essentially Ubuntu with the restricted components removed. It also uses most of the Ubuntu repositories by default, so it should get most of the normal Ubuntu updates. I'm not sure, though, so it may be prudent to ask on #gnewsense on Freenode.

3) If, for some reason, GNewSense is not satisfactory, the restricted modules have always been kept in a separate part of Ubuntu called *restricted* that is quite easy to remove. Problem solved.

4) If any of the above solutions/responses are not satisfactory, there is always Debian.

just because 90% of your system is open source, doesnt mean it all has to be. the whole point is choice, right? and part of the choice is going with open source or proprietary, whichever is better. if you going to keep going for open source, instead of going for what's best, you are only going to hold yourself back.

well, this is just my opinion, but i really feel that this whole proprietary vs open source thing is actually now starting to do us some harm. dont get me wrong, i'm all for open technologies, but there is a time and a place for everything.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
Once again you seem to have missed that Ubuntu has always had proprietary drivers. I'll quote it for you again--it was at the very beginning of the page you linked to: > **Ubuntu has included firmware, and used proprietary drivers since its inception.** That&#8217;s always been a slightly uncomfortable proposition, as Mako observed, but **it&#8217;s been true since the Warty Warthog.** Even Debian goes some way along that road with its inclusion of firmware and other non-free bits, suspending belief in the DFSG in this particular case. We discussed this at length in one of our earliest summits and settled on the hardware enablement vs apps boundary.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
[quote=greggh]But, I always assumed that those proprietary drivers weren't installed by default.[/quote] I don't know if that assumption is correct. From [the Ubuntu components page](http://www.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/components): > The restricted component is reserved for software that is very commonly used, and which is supported by the Ubuntu team even though it is not available under a completely free licence. Please note that it may not be possible to provide complete support for this software since we are unable to fix the software ourselves, but can only forward problem reports to the actual authors.

Some software from restricted will be installed on Ubuntu CDs but is clearly separated **to ensure that it is easy to remove.** We include this software because it is essential in order for Ubuntu to run on certain machines - typical examples are the binary drivers that some video card vendors publish, which are the only way for Ubuntu to run on those machines. By default, we will only use open source software **unless there is simply no other way to install Ubuntu.** The Ubuntu team works with such vendors to accelerate the open-sourcing of their software to ensure that as much software as possible is available under a Free licence.  That's been the description for as long as I can remember. It can be easily removed, but it is there by default.

---

### Post by Iandefor on 2006-11-29
> **greggh said:**
> Why has Mark decided that it's a bad thing to even give the user a choice? He hasn't. In fact, it's been made clear multiple times that it's trivially simple to remove the restricted software in question. You have the choice. It's just not presented during the system install.

Now, if you were to suddenly encounter something like a popup window saying "sorry, you can't uninstall those!" when you tried to uninstall the restricted modules... that would be taking away choice from the user. As it stands, it's just a single operation after you've installed Ubuntu and you never have to worry about proprietary modules again.

### Post by smoker on 2006-11-29
i'm a fairly recent user of linux and ubuntu, and probably not much brighter than an average computer user. when i first received my ubuntu disk, i ran it as a live cd, liked what i saw, and installed.

there was no thinking of politics done, no worries about binery/propriatory drivers, and if an option came up requiring an answer to this, i would've been stuck, as i wouldn't have known the difference, or cared then. all i wanted was the os to work on my system at least as good as my last os (windows). it did, infact it surpassed what i expected. i've now got rid of windows forever.

since then i have given other people copies of ubuntu, the few that have stuck with it are the few who installed it and it detected their hardware and ran with little or no problems.

now, reading the forums here, and at other linux sites, i begin to understand why some people are throwing the toys out the pram. but, in reality, if ubuntu hadn't been an easy install for me, i would probably still be using windows. so for me, anything that makes ubuntu even easier to install in the future is alright with me.

if i decide to get high-brow political about it in the future, as Mark stated in his column, "I&#8217;m very happy that it is possible (and straightforward!) to remove the non-free drivers from Ubuntu,"

cheers:-)

### Post by greggh on 2006-11-29
> By default, we will only use open source software unless there is simply no other way to install Ubuntu.

Well, Mark seems changing his mind on this. From the way I read what he recently wrote on his blog, the condition "simply no other way to install Ubuntu" no longer applies to having those proprietary drivers intalled by default. It seems now the reason Mark is giving to have them installed by default has changed to a matter of convenience.

> Q: Why has Mark decided that it's a bad thing to even give the user a choice?

A: He hasn't. In fact, it's been made clear multiple times that it's trivially simple to remove the restricted software in question. You have the choice. It's just not presented during the system install.

I don't want to play semantics, but you and Mark are twisting the reality of the situation. The user is not being given the choice of whether or not to have this stuff installed by default. We are only being given the choice of removing this stuff after it has been installed whether we wanted it there in the first place or not.

### Post by greggh on 2006-11-29
To all those saying in essense, "it's no big deal what crap the user is forced to have installed by default, as long as he can remove it later." I can only say that I and many others think that its important that the user be able to control what is installed by default in the first palce, not just having the ability to uninstall it later. I would even say that Mark Shuttleworth used to think this was an important distinction himself, or he would not have laid out in the past as part of Ubuntu's guiding principals...

"By default, we will only use open source software unless there is simply no other way to install Ubuntu."

He now seems to be saying that the conditions for having these proprietary pieces installed has changed from "no other way to install Ubuntu", to something else, convenience is the word I would now use. That's Mark's right to change his mind on this. But I don't think we should put blinders on, and ignore this critical change in direction of Ubuntu's philosophy. It is no longer the same "open" and "choice" distro that Mark once so strongly defined it as.

### Post by Titus A Duxass on 2006-11-29
By the way it is [COLOR="Red"][SIZE="7"]lose[/SIZE][/COLOR] control not loose control.

Lose - to be deprived of or cease to have or retain.

Loose - not firmly or tightly fixed in place.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
I had assumed it was the transitive verb *loose*: [quote=Random House Dictionary]20.	to let loose; free from bonds or restraint.
21.	to release, as from constraint, obligation, or penalty.
22.	Chiefly Nautical. to set free from fastening or attachment: to loose a boat from its moorings.
23.	to unfasten, undo, or untie, as a bond, fetter, or knot.
24.	to shoot; discharge; let fly: to loose missiles at the invaders.
25.	to make less tight; slacken or relax.
26.	to render less firmly fixed; lessen an attachment; loosen.[/quote]

### Post by adam.tropics on 2006-11-29
Zetetic

I really don't see how default inclusion equates to losing the freedom to choose, on any Linux distro. And as for the level of Paranoia, I mean what, you think ATI/AMD aren't making enough money, so to bump up the coffers they're gonna accumulate a collection of our email addresses to sell on? Or perhaps they're gonna demand subscriptions and cease to work if we don't pay, I mean come on.

That said, if this is important to you, try [Sabayon Linux]("http://www.sabayonlinux.org/"). It's Gentoo based, you'll love that, and they have a proprietary [driver policy you]("http://www.sabayonlinux.org/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=258&Itemid=2") might approve of.

> I want to clear out all your doubts about ATI and NVIDIA proprietary drivers inclusion.
Starting from version 3.1, we do not ship those binary blobs anymore but, during the Live boot, they are runtime linked (I call this: runtime linking) and the final kernel modules (nvidia.ko and fglrx.ko) are generated back.
If you do not agree with this default behavior,  you only need to add "noproprietary" to the boot parameters.

Either way, calm down, anxiety is contagious!

### Post by kripkenstein on 2006-11-29
UPDATE: Mark Shuttleworth posted on his [blog]("http://www.markshuttleworth.com/archives/84") about the binary NVIDIA/ATI drivers issue. He addresses the complexities of the matter there. Basically he justifies using binary drivers for video, but not binary applications in general. Worth reading, whether or not you agree with him.

No word on whether this will be an option during installation, or just done by default, though (but discussion continues on the mailing list, as mentioned before in this thread).

As for the 'anti-propietary people don't know about code' remarks from a few pages back, well all I have to say to that is 'bull'. There are many extremely vocal anti-propietary/closed-source people who are actively developing the very kernel you are currently using.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
> **zetetic said:**

Today he says it will be trivially but who assures you this will be true tomorrow?? Why does it have to be true tomorrow?

As I told you before, in Linux there is no vendor lock-in. If tomorrow you don't like the way Ubuntu does stuff, then tomorrow you can switch to Gentoo or Debian.

If, however, tomorrow my wife doesn't like the way Apple does stuff, what's she going to do with all the thousands of dollars' worth of stuff she's spent on Apple-only hardware and software? If she doesn't like Adobe, what is she going to do with all her InDesign files?

Linux gives you the freedom to choose. Don't like Ubuntu, use something else. There's no contract.

### Post by jr.gotti on 2006-11-29
> **Super King said:**
> Open-source zealots won't like this, but at the end of the day I think most people want a computer that "just works." If including proprietary drivers helps Ubuntu work better for more people, then I'm all for it.

Amen. I have installed linux on plenty of peoples computers for them. Only around 20% keep it. Why? Here's there response to that question;

"Nothing works."

At first more experienced users will laugh at that statement. With linux, and time, you can **make** everything work. But not everyone is like us. They don't want to do that.

When you install windows, what do you get? For the most part, a completely working system. (MP3's play, Flash is a click away, Shockwave is available, Graphics drivers preinstalled.)

I don't like Windows, and I don't use it. Not because it's closed source. I couldn't care less. If it was closed source without security holes, large pricetag, and viruses out the arse, **I would use windows.** But it's not. It's expensive, and cheap.

You have to understand people coming to linux are coming from Windows. (For the most part) They are used to having everything work. And when it doesn't, they get scared and run away.

If having more things work "right out of the box" can be achieved through the use of preinstalled proprietary drivers, then as the quoted poster, I am all for it.

As for the OP, I believe you are being slightly ridiculous. It's nice to have an OS built around a healthy ideal such as "Linux For Humans" and "Free Software For All!!!," but when following that regiment to the "T" means denying the advancment of the OS as a whole, then it's ridiculous. I'd rather have a working linux install then a non-working, but completely open source linux install.

Compare it to an ancient civilization if you will. They don't use electronics, cars, or anything like that. Outside this civilization everyone is going through a "technological boom," and advancing themselves through this technology. Eventually, out ancient civilization will die off due to there refusal to accept the oppurtunity to advance.

When will I stop using Ubuntu? Either when it starts charging, or when it becomes that "ancient civilization," and becomes rustic in comparison with other distros.

Let's not let petty convictions keep us from advancement.

> At this moment I'm already downloading Debian Etch. But I'm very new to Linux, I only use Linux for 5 ou 6 weeks, and so I don't know if I have the knowledge to use Debian. But as things are know it seems that using Ubuntu is out of question.

### Post by Brunellus on 2006-11-29
The distro market is open (unlike other sectors of OS life).  If the OP is concerned about binary-blob drivers to the extent of bomb-throwing rage, then he would be best served by moving to another, freer distribution--perhaps the FSF-blessed GNU-buntu derivative, or a rigorously pure install of Debian, or perhaps a new, utterly Free build of Linux From Scratch.

I'm not happy about binary-blob drivers either, but they make the difference to me between a usable, flexible system and having several kilos of scrap lying about the house.  

My general line--and one that seems to be the Ubuntu consensus--is Free wherever possible, non-Free only when necessary.

Also, in response to jr.gotti, above:  

There are many ways to progress.  Binary-blob drivers help us here and now.  Free drivers help us eventually.  The latter is preferable to the former.  Most of us tend to install Linux on the hardware we already have, so we gravitate towards binary drivers.  New purchases can be directed towards suitably-Free alternatives.  

It's not worth throwing bombs over.  There are more important things to worry about in life.

---

And to clarify, I don't mean to "throw bombs," I'm just sharing my views.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
Ubuntu is in an awkward and unique position.

There are other distributions that allow you a completely open source distribution. Debian, Gentoo, LFS, and Slackware come to mind (though Mark says even Debian has some proprietary firmware), but none of those has as its primary goal making inroads into the Windows-dominated desktop market.

Then, there are other distributions that seek to be genuine alternatives to Windows--PCLinuxOS, Linspire, Mepis, Blag--and have no qualms about including non-free software and, in fact, pride themselves on using proprietary software that will give new users the impression things "just work."

Ubuntu is caught in the middle. It *wants* to be open source but also wants to entice ex-Windows users. If this tension bothers you, I think you should go to one of the other sides. If you want something completely free, build it from the ground up using something like Gentoo. If you want something that "just works" and even Automatix seems inconvenient to you, use PCLinuxOS or Mepis.

I'll tell you the truth: I didn't come to Ubuntu for its technical superiority over other distros (in fact, I don't believe it *is* technically superior to other distros), nor did I come to it for its commitment (however fickle it appears now) to Free software. I came to Ubuntu for its community, and that's why I stay with it. The support here is incredible while also being friendly. Conventional wisdom says Gentoo forums have much better support and documentation but are also relatively unfriendly to new users, and my experience with Mepis users is that they're on the whole very friendly but unknowledgeable.

To those who use Free software exclusively (and if you've been using Ubuntu up until now, you don't belong in this group, as Mark pointed out on his webpage that Ubuntu has included non-free firmware since Warty), you don't have to use Ubuntu. You're free to leave at any time.

And this is why I don't care what direction Ubuntu heads. If it heads in a direction I can't morally abide by, I'll just switch to another distribution. There is no vendor lock-in in Linux.

I'll keep saying that until you get it: **There is no vendor lock-in in Linux.**

---

### Post by d3v1ant_0n3 on 2006-11-29
> **jr.gotti said:**
> 

When you install windows, what do you get? For the most part, a completely working system. (MP3's play, Flash is a click away, Shockwave is available, Graphics drivers preinstalled.)

I have to reinstall Windows on the kids' computer sometime soon. It's running slow as hell, is very bogged down, and generally suffers from 'been installed too long' disease.

And I'm not looking forward to it. Since it's a home build, I don't have a handy OEM disk. So when i install Windows, there will be no motherboard drivers, no graphics drivers, the internet won't work (wireless), my browser of choice (firefox) won't be installed, and there will be no useful software available. And the support? Hopeless. If they would use Ubuntu, I could just pop in the disk and leave it for 20 mins, and everything would work out of the box (except those nasty closed source graphics drivers!). But they like windows, and they choose to use lots of programs and games that are windows only. Their computer, their choice. 

But I'm in danger of dragging this topic off the rails.

So back to the point, WHY would Nvidia or ATI install malware infected drivers? What would be in it for them? Sony installed their rootkit as a way of cracking down on piracy. And it backfired. VERY badly for them. Despite publically apologising, replacing the cds and removing the rootkit installing stuff from their cds, many many people have sworn never to buy their cds again. And this is cds, that cost $10-$20. When you buy a graphics card, you tend to spend a lot more than that. And it's in the vendors interest to want you to buy from them again. Including malware with their drivers would be suicide. They don't keep their drivers closed source so they can slip in nasties, they do it to stop the competition seeing what they do with their drivers, so neither company can get the upper hand. Or thats how I see it at least.

If you don't want closed source, don't buy from a vendor who only supplies closed source drivers. If you don't want closed source drivers, uninstall them. If you don't like a distro that has closed source binaries included, use another distro.

Alternatively, get one of [THESE](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abacus). Not sure if they're covered under GPL tho'.

### Post by Henry Rayker on 2006-11-29
Kristen's point, on this, is rather valid, in the sense that this is a potential direct result from closed source drivers, however, there is a large amount of support behind the drivers coming pre-installed.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-29
I think someone may have alluded to this before, but it bears repeating:

If you're using Nvidia hardware, you are supporting Nvidia. Nvidia doesn't get hurt in any way by you using their hardware and then installing only open source drivers. They couldn't care less if you use open source drivers. They are hardware manufacturers.

If you want to promote open source, buy hardware from companies that provide open source drivers or release the code for their drivers. Money talks.

### Post by AndyCooll on 2006-11-29
> **Brunellus said:**
> I'm not happy about binary-blob drivers either ...  

My general line--and one that seems to be the Ubuntu consensus--is Free wherever possible, non-Free only when necessary.

This basically sums up my viewpoint too. 

I would have preferred to be presented with options in truth, however the choice has been made.

The open-source philosophy is important to me and I will choose open-source over proprietary where possible. However I also want everything to "just work", so if necessary I will compromise and use a little proprietary stuff too. 

It will be a great day when we can have a frontline 100% open-source Linux distro where everything works with everything out of the box ...but we're not yet at that stage. Ubuntu out of the box, Debian out of the box, Gnewsense etc all show this. Invariably we then find ourselves installing some proprietary driver or codec to get a certain application to work.
Some folk are able to run a system without any proprietary stuff, and are willing to change their software usage habits go ensure this stays the case. And I'm pleased for them. Most of us however make compromises, whether reluctantly or simply because they couldn't care less about the open/closed issue.

:cool:

### Post by engla on 2006-11-29
The disappointment with Ubuntu is big for many reasons, but I think it is especially because so far Ubuntu has been a great project. Ubuntu has been true and in many ways unique. Ubuntu has done many great things.

From this, many people, like me, are hoping for still great things to come. One of these great things would be to reject proprietary drivers in symbol/standoff areas, and in the meantime fund alternative approaches (like nouvaeu driver or some completely alternative graphics card project).

Now, what Ubuntu is doing is perhaps not wrong for its users, but I (and others) were hoping for other news, for a direction we could continue to push on.

Having been on GNU/Linux for just a short while (1,5 years), I have still seen tremendous improvement in driver support and general usability. I genuinely believe that for my and your future computer to work perfectly in 10- 15 years, free drivers are crucial. Without establishing the openness on the market and all the users and producers on it, we have won nothing.

Ubuntu is large and successful and could do such things. That's why I and others are disappointed now.

### Post by givré on 2006-11-29
Ok this clarified the situation (say me if i'm wrong) :
[http://damien.bergamini.free.fr/ipw/](http://damien.bergamini.free.fr/ipw/)
```
You may have noticed that the Linux drivers developed by Intel® are under GPL and the ipw
and iwi drivers are not. The ipw and iwi drivers are NOT ports of the Linux drivers but are
complete re-writes (from scratch). Absolutely no code has been taken from Intel® which does not
support neither officially nor unofficially these drivers in any way.

```
So what i think is just that intel provided a GPL driver for linux for their Intel® PRO/Wireless 2100/2200BG/2915ABG/3945ABG cards, but not for *BSD platform. So they simply write one from scratch for their need.
And The firmwire situation is the same for both plateform : binary-only.

### Post by chojin on 2006-12-08
I realize this is page 11 and nobody is likely reading this anymore. But, as a user who has spent a long time wrestling with ubuntu, I'm going to cut right in and give you the cold, hard truth.

Right now, linux (and therefore ubuntu) is a hobby OS. I have never installed or known anybody who has ever installed linux and had a trouble free experience, on any distro, ever. I have installed the same distro, same version, but different architecture (ie. 64 bit vs 32 bit distros) which should have come from the same code, and had wildly different problems with both. You would really only install this if you have a passion for computers, are already well versed with them, or like me, you find windows abhorrently hostile.

I know many people are thinking 'well it must be your lack of understanding of linux'... and that's true. But why should it take 100 times the knowledge and research to learn linux as the 'big two' other OS'es in the mainstream today.

Open source has gotten so caught up in trying to be 'right' that it's forgotten how to be useful to people. I can see so much benefit in the concept, but the application of it is just... abismal.

I would find it amusing if it weren't so frustrating that ubuntu tries to pretend it is 'for humans' and about 'intuitiveness', when, every user under the sun has a 3D card these days, listens to mp3s, and watches flash content (ie. youtube, etc). And you have to dig into the OS and need a network connection to do any of this. I agree that windows doesn't make this easy either, but it's a lot easier than with linux and besides - who uses windows as a benchmark anyway? Only purists are going to switch to an OS that is 'sort of as good', joe user doesn't even KNOW what the GPL is, and he's never going to, because he doesn't care. This is 99% of people out there.

Include the driver. Talk to the manufacturers. Do it immediately and don't stop there. Sure it would be nice to have open source versions, but the manufacturers aren't interested. The world isn't GPL friendly, and it is a fantastical ideal that is inhibiting the growth of what could be a wonderful time in our technological existence.

Now that microsoft are showing their true colors with vista, and trying to impede and trap the user at every turn, people are looking for an alternative. But it won't be linux unless there's a BIG change, and ubuntu really lives up to the word is has chosen as its title, being about people, not idealistic politics.

---

### Post by kripkenstein on 2006-12-08
Well, the poll has gone on for a long time. The numbers seems stable to me.

It seems that 15% are flat-out against including NVidia/ATI drivers by default; they want things to stay as they are. Many of these, if not all, see this as an ethical issue. Without saying which side of the issue I am on, I think the following is quite clear: **Ubuntu needs to be prepared to lose as many as 15% of its users because of this issue**. The chance of this occurring cannot be ignored. Is Mr. Shuttleworth willing to take the gamble? It seems so, for now.

Furthermore, the number may be even larger, as over 50% voted to have an *option* to install binary drivers. If in Feisty the drivers are installed by default - and not optionally - then part of the 50% may be offended enough to reconsider their choice of distro. Of course, this is just interpretation of the survey. But I don't think it can easily be ignored.

---

### Post by The Grum on 2006-12-08
Has anyone actually **read** the specs lately (as in the last week or so)?

[AcceleratedX]("https://wiki.ubuntu.com/AcceleratedX")


> 
Open Source Video Drivers

    *

      The opensource 'nv' driver does not support 3D acceleration or Composite, but will be installed by default, and used on the following use cases:
          o

            NVIDIA users with current video boards (supported via the 'nvidia' driver) will have the chance to use this driver as an alternative to the proprietary driver
          o

            NVIDIA users with legacy video boards (supported via the 'nvidia_legacy' driver) will have the 'nv' driver enabled by default, due to limited support of the legacy proprietary driver
    *

      The 'nv' open source has no Composite or 3D acceleration support, and thus, the experimental Nouveau 2D/3D driver will be offered as a third alternative for users with NVIDIA boards. This driver is currently considered under development, and will be offered on a best-effort basis. It is considered functional for various use cases, though. The Nouveau driver *will* also be considered as an alternative as default NVIDIA driver for Feisty+1, in case it is stable enough in six months from now
    *

      The Open Source 'ATI' driver will be enabled by default for any ati video board it supports (this excludes the newest X1300-X1950, but all others should work, 2D and 3D, except for possible bugs or required special options)
    *

      Specific options for all open source video drivers will be researched during development period, and will be enabled as required during xorg.conf creation (this is explained in detail on the "Implementation/Code" section below)


So, if you have an older ATI card, you get the Free "ati" driver by default. If you have a newer one, you get the binary (and no composite). If you have a legacy NVidia, you can have "nv" or Nouveau. If you have a newer NVidia, you can have the binary or Nouveau.

I dont see anything wrong with this approach. The way I see it, its using the binary as a temporary placeholder so we can build up beryl/compiz and get that stable. When a Free 3D driver comes along that will work reliably enough, the binaries will disappear.

Whats wrong with that?

---

### Post by JeevesBond on 2006-12-10
I think this [doomsday scenario](http://lwn.net/Articles/162686/) sums the argument up well. This isn't exactly the same issue, but I believe the outcomes could be very similar.

Salient points are:
[list=1]
[*]Binary blobs deemed acceptable
[*]Those hardware manufacturers who provide FLOSS or partly open drivers have no reason to continue doing so. In fact they see themselves at a competitive disadvantage as they have not included special features in open drivers for fear of competitors taking their ideas. Their competitors are able to provide special features for their products via binary drivers.
[*]Practically all hardware manufacturers switch to producing binary drivers. They only support a few 'Enterprise' distros like SLED and RHEL.
[*]New kernel comes out. No-one can use it because it breaks binary compatibility with drivers. Development on Linux can only be done by people with older machines with open source drivers that can be compiled against newer kernel.
[*]Bug appears that requires new kernel, but hardware manufacturers can't be bothered to release new drivers. The release cycle is 'too fast' for their liking.
[*]Exploit uses bug to gain access to web servers. Apache loses market share, everyone switches to Microsoft ServerThingy 2057 (or whatever it's called).
[/list]

Those who accuse us of going on some moral crusade clearly don't have an understanding of what is at stake. You talk of choice, yet accepting binary drivers could destroy that choice. This could mean an end to any free drivers ('why bother', thinks the manufacturer) and an end to free distributions that rely upon them (Debian, openSUSE etc.)

To paraphrase a great man:
[quote=Benjamin Franklin (if he were here now)]Those who would give up Essential Liberty to purchase a little Ease-of-installation, deserve neither Liberty nor Ease-of-installation.[/quote]

**Big fat caveat:** this is very over-simplified for those who don't know the technical details. This is a difficult issue to grasp, if I have it completely wrong then do correct me. :)

---

### Post by deanlinkous on 2006-12-10
> **bob_c_b said:**
> First, as others have posted before, [Linux != WIndows]("http://linux.oneandoneis2.org/LNW.htm"), nor should it try to. Windows is a grossly inferior product. It skates by on ubiquity and compatibility, but that compatibility is born of monopolistic and (if not illegal) at least unethical business practices. You are not likely to truly attract a lot of users to Linux promising ease of use based on one driver/blob/package; compromising your values on this issue is truly the top of the slippery slope.

The notion that including non-free drivers will somehow cause a huge Linux growth spurt is unrealistic at best. Any potential Linux user who installs Ubuntu based purely on desktop effects and transparency will likely immediately switch back to Windows when the next episode of Half Life won't work or they can't install iTunes. Linux is about learning as much as using, simplify the install and improve the documentation but don't remove the necessity to understand.

A good number of us (long time Linux users) switched to this distro based on Mark's initial claim of sticking to the letter of F/OSS philosophy and seeming very sincere. I know there are some proprietary blobs currently in the installer, but those are mostly born of necessity. Better to simplify the install process for additional proprietary blobs (wireless, video, etc...) than to chip away at the foundation. Fedora does okay with this method and the don't have nearly the quality of community that Ubuntu does.

Throughout these discussions on this topic I see little winks and nudges about "crusaders" and "users who want to change the world" as if that is a bad thing? If it weren't for free software advocates trying to change the world you wouldn't have Ubuntu, so don't be so quick to disregard this philosophy  or declare your pragmatism trumps idealism.

nicely stated!

### Post by BuffaloX on 2006-12-10
> **luca.b said:**
> In Ubuntu, the drivers are linked at boot, therefore there is no problem with GPL, unlike Kororaa that had them already linked.

[B]It doesn't matter when or where proprietary drivers are linked.
It's against the GPL license.[/B]

But if you make the linking happen on the users system, it is the users responsibility, and therefore technically the user that breaks the license.
However I'm pretty sure that won't hold in court. The average user doesn't know a thing about this. So it's really also Ubuntu/Nvidea that's responsible.

The only reason ATI Nvidea and others can continue to make proprietary drivers to Linux, is because nobody stops them.
One problem in taking this to court, is how to figure out a proper compensation, how do you compensate for loss, when we are talking free software?

Seems like Shuttleworth only means free as not pay for, when he says Ubuntu will stay free. If that's the case, I think Ubuntu will fade away pretty quickly. I don't think many devs wish to work with such a distro, and it will be impossible to have good relations with the rest of the community.

I don't think (like most i guess) 3d drivers will mean mass migration to Linux. But maybe it will win some over, and give Linux a little nudge further in general user acceptance. I just don't see how this is done in any way, that is acceptable and respectful towards the kernel devs.
But if Ubuntu can walk that fine line, best of wishes...
I don't think a warning at installation quite cuts it.

---

### Post by FyreBrand on 2006-12-11
> **technodigifreak said:**
> I voted no.  I agree with Fyre.  Give us the option of installing the propietary stuff, don't force it on us.  This kind of crap is why I've moved to linux in the first place.  To avoid lock in.  By automatically including these binary drivers we must assume that they are inherently **INSECURE**, because we cannot verify their status ourselves.  I will never use a Linux with propietary drivers on a production machine. **PERIOD**.

Dapper was/is awesome.  Edgy is great.  Fiesty Fawn may just as well be called ***Ubuntu Vista***.To clarify:  I think if a user chooses the most simple and automated form of install the binary blobs should be installed by default.  This goes for any driver that makes the system run smoother not just video drivers.  A large portion of the general public has a difficult time with understanding some of these concepts and they really shouldn't have to.

I would like it such that when a user chooses an advanced install option, for example configuring your own partitions, that you are then presented with choices for installing driver support.

To be clear I'm a user of proprietary hardware drivers.  I'm a supporter of an easier install and configuration of these drivers.  But I'm also a supporter of making that option just as clear and easy for those who *don't* want that in their system.  I find it ridiculous to to infight and ridicule those who have a differing software philosophy from any spectrum.  Freedom of choice is important and divisive arguing accomplishes nothing.  Just as an oversimplified historical reminder two things that shattered UNIX collaboration and allowed Microsoft to dominate the desktop OS was 1) ignoring the common home user and 2) infighting and divisiveness.[1,2]

1. [Wikipedia article - UNIX wars]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNIX_wars"): [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNIX_wars](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNIX_wars)
2. Eric S. Raymond, ["The Art of Unix Programming", Chapter 2]("http://catb.org/esr/writings/taoup/html/historychapter.html"), online current: [http://catb.org/esr/writings/taoup/html/historychapter.html](http://catb.org/esr/writings/taoup/html/historychapter.html)

### Post by deanlinkous on 2006-12-11
Well, free software ensures sharing of code so that no matter how much in-fighting, divisiveness, and disagreement may occur no shattering can occur, nothing proprietary can be made from it, nobody can claim it as their "own" so wars cannot occur. That is why free software is much more important to me than just open source software.

### Post by FyreBrand on 2006-12-11
> **deanlinkous said:**
> Well, free software ensures sharing of code so that no matter how much in-fighting, divisiveness, and disagreement may occur no shattering can occur, nothing proprietary can be made from it, nobody can claim it as their "own" so wars cannot occur. That is why free software is much more important to me than just open source software.I think free software ensures the *idea* of collaboration and sharing.  I think we know the practice of that isn't anywhere near the ideal else we wouldn't have quite as many forks as we do (compiz and beryl are good recent examples).  That's not a bad thing though.  A lot of innovation can come from differing opinions and ideas.

I don't think closed software is necessarily the best option either.  I also don't think belligerent brute force tactics is the way to get hardware vendors to open their code.  I do think diplomacy and communication is a much much better approach.

I also think that when using free and open source drivers is that important to you that you should be able to have that option at your install.  I don't think you should have to install the proprietary blobs and then purge them to install the free drivers.  That doesn't make any sense to me at all.

I don't know if it's realistic to have a modified installer ready by Feisty but hopefully by Feisty+1 this will be the standard approach to installing hardware drivers.  I think having the option at install could help this become more of a non-issue.

### Post by flange on 2006-12-11
> **dpw2atox said:**
> I use what works and think that its just dumb to want a crippled computer so you can be elite and tout how your pc is all free software.

It's not really about elitism as much as principles.  Some people highly value free (as in speech) software.  To call that mindset elitist is a bit naive.  The question of the thread asks what people value more:  principle or practicality, and where people draw that line.  Obviously you value practicality more than principle (or maybe you don't understand the principle at risk), which is fine.

I tend to be a little on the principle side of the middle.  If a F/OSS solution will not work for me, I will use a proprietary one because I want to complete my task.  If a reasonably comparable F/OSS solution exists, I will make an effort to use that over a similar proprietary one.

In this specific case, I use the nVidia binary drivers to power Beryl, because F/OSS nVidia drivers don't do the job well enough for me yet.  When a F/OSS solution is comparable to what I'm using now, you'll likely see me change over.

### Post by BuffaloX on 2006-12-11
> **JeevesBond said:**
> I think this [doomsday scenario](http://lwn.net/Articles/162686/) sums the argument up well. This isn't exactly the same issue, but I believe the outcomes could be very similar.

Salient points are:
[list=1]
[*]Binary blobs deemed acceptable
[*]Those hardware manufacturers who provide FLOSS or partly open drivers have no reason to continue doing so. In fact they see themselves at a competitive disadvantage as they have not included special features in open drivers for fear of competitors taking their ideas. Their competitors are able to provide special features for their products via binary drivers.
[*]Practically all hardware manufacturers switch to producing binary drivers. They only support a few 'Enterprise' distros like SLED and RHEL.
[*]New kernel comes out. No-one can use it because it breaks binary compatibility with drivers. Development on Linux can only be done by people with older machines with open source drivers that can be compiled against newer kernel.
[*]Bug appears that requires new kernel, but hardware manufacturers can't be bothered to release new drivers. The release cycle is 'too fast' for their liking.
[*]Exploit uses bug to gain access to web servers. Apache loses market share, everyone switches to Microsoft ServerThingy 2057 (or whatever it's called).
[/list]

Those who accuse us of going on some moral crusade clearly don't have an understanding of what is at stake. You talk of choice, yet accepting binary drivers could destroy that choice. This could mean an end to any free drivers ('why bother', thinks the manufacturer) and an end to free distributions that rely upon them (Debian, openSUSE etc.)

To paraphrase a great man:
  Originally Posted by Benjamin Franklin (if he were here now)
Those who would give up Essential Liberty to purchase a little Ease-of-installation, deserve neither Liberty nor Ease-of-installation.

**Big fat caveat:** this is very over-simplified for those who don't know the technical details. This is a difficult issue to grasp, if I have it completely wrong then do correct me. :)

I wish you were wrong, so we could just go ahead, unconcerned about the consequences.
But I'm afraid you are right.

You're confusing what the OP means by the word "free".  You're using it in the context of free beer.  The OP means free speech.  What the free driver advocates are really after is the source code, rather than not having to pay money.

I agree with you about paying for the cost of development time.  It takes companies to make hardware, and companies have to make money.  I have no problem paying nVidia for the time they spend developing a video card that I want to use.  I also understand that developing a driver is part of that cost.

What free (as in speech) software advocates want is the ability to inspect and modify the software that runs on the hardware they buy.  With that kind of free software, a user is no longer dependent on a company or anyone else for updates/fixes to the software.  A community can form to fill the need.  Heck, a community formed behind a company can even help take some of the development load off to make producing the hardware less expensive.

### Post by BuffaloX on 2006-12-11
The quote is shortened a little, I try to capture the essence, please read above for entire post.

> **josys36 said:**
> I just find it so stupid to have a completely free system that we can't use.

Jason

I think what you say, is what many people feel. It's OK to pay for software, because people work hard to make it, they should be paid for their work. And its better to pay for a system that works, than getting one that doesn't for free.
You are absolutely right on all points, and most software is build on that idea, and you are free to use those instead of Linux.

BUT:

Linux is another kind of system, originally by and for computer students.
The basic idea was to have a system, which was free, So students could learn about and work together on everything in the system.
This turned out to be such a powerful idea, that it outgrew any original plans the original author had.
Fortunately it was released with the GPL license attached, which means that everybody is free to use change and spread it. 
BUT if you want to change it, you must share your changes.

It's like: "I show you my code, and you show me yours."
If you don't it's like pissing on every Linux developer, who shared his or her code.
It's the cornerstone of all Linux development.
There are also technical reasons why Linux drivers should not be closed.

You can read more about this here:
[http://www.kroah.com/log/linux/ols_2006_keynote.html]("http://www.kroah.com/log/linux/ols_2006_keynote.html")

### Post by yorick on 2006-12-12
I didn't have time to read the whole thread. but I want to tell you my oppinions. I hope I'm not repeating something already said.

My take is when you buy a graphics card you are buying the hardware (or at least the hardware+software package). The companies are in the bussiness of making hardware. They provide the drivers for the hardware. If they have in the hardware something unique, something that puts them ahead of the competition, they cannot simply  open up the specification and let everyone copy what they have. If you want the latest card with the greatest features, then you have to contend with whatever way the manufacturer decides to provide drivers for it.

If the card is older, and there is nothing unique (advanced over the competition) to it anymore, then I think the manufacturer must open up the driver/card specs, so everyone can improve it. Until then, I think expecting open source drivers is unrealistic. 

So I understand it when they provide closed FREE (as in beer) drivers, and I am happy if the hardware (latest card) I bought works great with what they provide. After all, they are the ones that also write the driver for Windows, which also isn't open source...

The best way to have a great COMPLETELY FREE (as in speech) graphics card is to have it developed open source from the start (from the design to the drivers for it). I think I saw somewhere right in these forums there is already a project like this underway. Until that, we have to contend with what is available from the manufacturers.

Now, about the Ubuntu closed drivers by default. I think the user should be given a choice at installation, with expalanations about what (s)he can choose. If there is available a good open source driver for the card it should default to that, if not it should default to the manufacturer's driver. 

Here's to hoping we will get a completely FOSS latest & greatest graphics card soon. :toasts_a_glass_of_wine:

---
