### Post by leech on 2006-12-12
Not that my thoughts are going to matter much, I'll throw them in anyhow.

1) Ubuntu is striving for the Desktop.

In essence this means that it should try to support video cards as much as possible, who here would buy a $400+ video card and not be able to use it to the fullest?  Not all people who buy gaming computers will have the know-how to install the nvidia-glx drivers, granted I know not many people who are hard core gamers will run Linux by itself, but if we want more people to boot into Linux than Windows, it'll have to have a much 'cooler' desktop.

If you want "Free", install Debian.  Etch is almost released and it has newer core packages than even Edgy, though it's still at Gnome 2.14 unfortunately (mostly due to GTK 2.10 bugs).  

2) Ubuntu already uses closed firmware / drivers.

As already pointed out, Ubuntu already contains closed firmware or drivers for various wireless cards and other devices.  A good example is that when I installed Debian Etch on my Laptop, I still had to download the ipw2200 firmware for my Intel wireless card (which by the way does have free drivers which are in Debian, but they still require the "non-free" firmware, which is NOT in Debian.)  Apparently Debian even had many discussions on whether or not to remove any and all packages that contained any binary blobs at all.  Debian truly is working towards a completely "Free" Operating System.

Why is it that people will make exceptions where their network hardware is concerned over their video hardware?  Sure I guess one could argue that without the binary blob firmwares that their hardware wouldn't work at all, but their video card does work with the free driver.  Vesa or simple 2D display isn't what a lot of people would define as 'work' though.  Again, not enabling the features that were meant for their expensive video hardware in Linux is just another reason for them to stay in Windows.

3) OEM installs.

Let's face it, on any computer that a shop is going to sell to the public, they're probably going to be killed off pretty quickly if they don't supply the full capability for their customers.  Granted some would argue that a shop could be sued by the copyright holders of the Linux kernel because they linked non-GPL software with it, but actually that's not how the nvidia kernel works (at least from many of the things I read said.)  From what I gathered from the whole Kororaa discussion was that nVidia uses a sort of translator  that is LGPL that talks to the binary blob, which is the same binary blob used in Windows, Solaris and FreeBSD.  Hence how they can pull off "unified drivers."  Even Linus himself was fine with the way that nVidia drivers hooked into the kernel.  The real part of the license deals with 'derived work' which is really not solidly set in stone on what that means.

4) Nouveau

Until this is a viable solution for at least the nVidia cards, then binary is basically about all we have.  ATI at least have support for their older cards.  

5) Lawsuits.  

From what I've gathered from different sources, nVidia wanted to open source their drivers or release their specs, but apparently Intel stopped them.  Intel has a patent on something to do with AGP, so neither ATI nor nVidia can open source their drivers because they have Intel's code inside it.


Now don't quote me on any of the above, it's mostly what I've read and the opinion of someone extremely tired.  I really didn't vote, 'cause I know polls like this really are pointless and if the developers of Ubuntu have chosen to make this decision, then all the power to them.  The majority of distributions out there are going this route.  There will always be those that are "Free" Like Debian, etc.  

As a reply to someone saying they had no problem with paying for a driver for their hardware.  I have serious issue with it.  Why?  Because I already paid for the driver by buying their hardware!  Vendors charging for hardware drivers separately from the hardware itself would be like fast food joints charging for their ketchup!  It comes with the damned fries!

Leech

---

### Post by flange on 2006-12-12
> **leech said:**
> From what I've gathered from different sources, nVidia wanted to open source their drivers or release their specs, but apparently Intel stopped them.  Intel has a patent on something to do with AGP, so neither ATI nor nVidia can open source their drivers because they have Intel's code inside it.

Does that mean we might see open source drivers now that the market is moving to PCI Express?

> As a reply to someone saying they had no problem with paying for a driver for their hardware.  I have serious issue with it.  Why?  Because I already paid for the driver by buying their hardware!  Vendors charging for hardware drivers separately from the hardware itself would be like fast food joints charging for their ketchup!  It comes with the damned fries!

I wasn't implying that we should pay hardware vendors separately for their drivers.  It's an included cost in the price of the hardware.  You pay for ketchup when you buy the fries, and you pay for the drivers when you buy the hardware.  They're all included costs.

What I *was* saying is that I don't have a problem with companies including that cost in the price of the hardware.  It's part of the development cost, and it's only fair that customers should be expected to pay if they want to use the product.  Programmers need to eat, too!

### Post by MadMan2k on 2006-12-12
> **leech said:**
> 
Why is it that people will make exceptions where their network hardware is concerned over their video hardware?
Firmware is a different issue, since it runs only on the wlan chip, so essentially the binary blob is only being bassed by.
Graphics drivers on the other hand are huge binary blobs which run in kernel space and also replace partly the MESA and DRI/ DRM subsystems.

the next logical step would be replace firefox by opera since the latter has a better page rendering at the cost of "just" binary avaibility.

### Post by flange on 2006-12-12
> **josys36 said:**
> So this begs the question of what is Linux here for? It is just to have a play pen for computer techies that don't want to run widnows? Is it for folks who just plan hate windows and will run OS/2 to be rid of it? Or, is it a useful alternative to winodws? If the answer is number 3 then it has to work. It just plain has to work.

Awesome question.

The answer depends on who you ask, and all three are likely responses.

As a distro, I would say Ubuntu strives for #3.  Ubuntu's bug #1 states that Microsoft has a majority market share and Ubuntu wants to change that.

[Bug #1]("https://launchpad.net/bug1.html")

I personally don't care if Windows is ever displaced as the majority.  I want Linux to have enough marketshare that the market ensures it can interoperate with Linux.  I feel the same way about Firefox and IE.  As long as Firefox and alternative browsers have enough share to force web developers to take them into consideration, I could care less what anyone else uses.

I don't really think any Linux distro should "sell out" to gain users.  Users who come to Linux for a feature set rather than a philosophy will just leave for another platform if/when its feature set is better.  I think competing with propietary systems based on a feature set is a losing proposition for a Linux distro.

The thing that propietary systems can never compete with is the F/OSS philosohpy.  What you're seeing in this thread when you see people ask Ubuntu to *not include something that would lead to more/better features*, is people that believe very much in the principles of F/OSS.

(As a counter to my own belief, you could argue that the best strategy is to get a user on to Linux anyway you can, and then indoctrinate as many as possible to F/OSS principles later on.)

It's always a touchy and controversial subject among the Linux community, especially when many are here because they are fed up with Windows and want something "better".  In a way, that's why we're all here, we just don't all agree on what "better" means.

---

### Post by aent on 2006-12-12
> **FyreBrand said:**
> They should be included in the Live CD by default and the setup should ask the user whether or not they want the binary drivers installed.

For me I would love to have that option as I always install the NVIDIA drivers.

I would love to use the open drivers but they don't work good enough for me right now.  I don't use fancy desktop environments like compiz or beryl so it has nothing to do with that.  They are just slow and buggy and not quite ready for everyday use, at least not for me.

I find it interesting that proponents of using only foss drivers suggest paying the developers.  Asking the end user to spend more money for a driver for hardware they already paid for seems odd to me.  Why wouldn't I just use NVIDIA's driver?  That's what I paid for when I bought their card.

I think a better idea than telling NVIDIA and ATI to piss off is to figure out a way to create an environment in the Linux world where they can and will want to open their drivers.  Or open them again as I think for a time NVIDIA or ATI did have some open code until MS slammed their fingers in the door.

Anyways I don't think fighting the hardware vendors will produce a good solution.

I think fighting the hardware vendors will produce a great solution, however, we just need a great strategy to do it. Continuing to buy their products and then protesting it isn't going to work. Its like saying "target shouldn't be in my city" while continuing to shop there. What needs to be done is giving out the message to their customers when they use the ubuntu product that they're using a product from a troublesome company, but we're still managing to support them. If Ubuntu wants to gain marketshare, we have to do everything we can to help the end user, which means supporting all of their hardware to its full potential, which is currently impossible without binary drivers. However, when we install binary drivers, we can and SHOULD tell the user, "hey, next time you're buying a computer, look for intel integrated graphics, as they are releasing drivers that we are able to support easily, but nvidia won't do the same"... Users will be very happy with that message, as they don't need to manually install any drivers, but they'll probably also want to take the good advice of Ubuntu when buying their next computer. nvidia and ATI will NOT like that message, and doing stuff like that is what will really HURT them and encourage them to do everything they can to open up their drivers as much as possible. Protesting isn't going to work with them, we've already found that out from all of the wireless manufacturers, video cards, as well as many other products. A new strategy needs to be taken, and I hope Ubuntu uses its popularity and power to use this new strategy.

To use your analogy, imagine Target is the only grocery store in town.  You have to make a choice between boycotting Targest and feeding your family.  I suppose you could always move to a different town, but that's still a massive undertaking for most of us.

To bring this back to the computing world, my paycheck depends on my use of computers.  I either have to a) buy and complain, b) boycott and not get paid, or c) find a new job.  Based on my own priorities, I choose option A.  If enough of us choose option A, then graphics manufacturers will either 1) cave to the pressure, or 2) someone entirely new will release a market oppurtunity and fill the need, giving us what we want anyway.  The key problem is getting enough of us to understand the issue and file the complaint.

A previous poster noted the possiblity of patent complications with the AGP bus.  If that's true, then it throws a pretty big wrench into what I've said above.  The market oppurtunity there comes in providing a new bus standard that allows open source graphics drivers.  *That* will be a lot harder to accomplish.

### Post by Artemis3 on 2006-12-13
You cannot claim your distribution is free if you include and default the use of non free software.

Ubuntu should not be considered free software, and now that they are defaulting to binary blobs, it is a high risk to the community.

It is not true that manufacturers can't open their specs, in fact its a minority who do not; most likely because they use patented tecnologies without permission, not because they have any "secrets" to "protect".

I am hoping AMD will open the specs to program GPUs as they have told. This will strangle nVidia like they deserve, maybe then Intel will buy them and open that hardware too.

Shuttleworth's attitude is pretty bad and stupid. In one side refusing what everyone wants: mp3/mp4 codecs, on the other putting rubbish binary blobs defaulting them on install. The ideals behind ubuntu are gone.

So why not be sincere, and let the codecs, opera, flash, etc enabled as well? With ubuntu you seem to get the worst of two sides. Are we supposed to get either gNewSense or Linux Mint now? 

I'm considering moving to another distro; maybe gentoo: plenty of packages, always updated.

> **http://www.openbsd.org/lyrics.html]

OpenBSD emphasizes security. It also emphasizes openness. All the code is there for all to see. Blobs are vendor-compiled binary drivers without any source code. Hardware makers like them because they obscure the details of how to make their hardware work. They hide bugs and workarounds for bugs. Newer versions of blobs can weaken support for older hardware and motivate people to buy new hardware.

Blobs are expedient. Many other open source operating systems cheerfully incorporate them said:**
>     [*] Blobs can be 'de-supported' by vendors at any time.

    [*] Blobs cannot be supported by developers.

    [*] Blobs cannot be fixed by developers.

    [*] Blobs cannot be improved.

    [*] Blobs cannot be audited.

    [*] Blobs are specific to an architecture, thus less portable.

    [*] Blobs are quite often massively bloated.[/list]
This release, like every OpenBSD release, contains OpenBSD and its source code. It runs on a wide variety of hardware. It contains many new features and improvements. OpenBSD does attempt to convince vendors to release documentation, and often reverse-engineers around the need for blobs. OpenBSD remains blob-free. Anyone can look at it, assess it, improve it. If it breaks, it can be fixed.

Can you still call "free" a distro which includes and enables blobs by default? Be honest, stop lying.

---

### Post by kripkenstein on 2006-12-14
UPDATE: The Linux kernel devs have been discussing banning binary drivers in the kernel. (The conclusion appears to be that they won't, not because they like binary drivers - they hate them - but because the legal means to ban them, as well as the ethics behind doing so, is suspect, in their eyes.) Interestingly, the issue of binary drivers in the next Ubuntu release was [mentioned]("http://article.gmane.org/gmane.linux.kernel/475834"): 

> The Ubuntu feisty fawn mess was a dangerous warning bell of where we're going. If we don't stand up at some point, and ban binary drivers, we will, I fear, end up with an unsustainable ecosystem for Linux when binary drivers become pervasive. I don't want to see Linux destroyed like that.

---

### Post by Artemis3 on 2006-12-14
Interesting quotes:

[quote=Ben Collins]Here's the list of proprietary drivers that are in Ubuntu's restricted
modules package:

	madwifi (closed hal implementation, being replaced in openhal)
	fritz
	ati
	nvidia
	ltmodem (does that even still work?)
	ipw3945d (not a kernel module, but just the daemon)[/quote]

[quote=David Woodhouse]closed code is undebuggable,
unportable, unimprovable, unworkable. It's a detriment to the whole
system.[/quote]

[quote=Linus Torvalds]I don't like binary modules. I refuse to support them, and if it turns out 
that the module was written using Linux code, and just for Linux, I htink 
that's a _clear_ copyright violation, and that binary module is obviously 
a license violation.[/quote]

[quote=Linus Torvalds]You can still do whatever you want, but people who support the resulting 
mess know that they shouldn't.

		Linus[/quote]

[quote=Greg KH]And yes, it is crap that I deal with every day due to the lovely grey
area that is Linux kernel module licensing these days.  I have customers
that demand we support them despite them mixing three and more different
closed source kernel modules at once and getting upset that I have no
way to help them out.  I have loony video tweakers that hand edit kernel
oopses to try to hide the fact that they are using a binary module
bigger than the sum of the whole kernel and demand that our group fix
their suspend/resume issue for them.  I see executives who say one thing
to the community and then turn around and overrule them just because
someone made a horrible purchasing decision on the brand of laptop wifi
card that they purchased.  I see lawyers who have their hands tied by
attorney-client rules and can not speak out in public for how they
really feel about licenses and how to interpret them.[/quote]

[quote=Greg KH]And in the midst of all of that are the poor users who have no idea who
to listen to.  They don't know what is going on, they "just want to use
their hardware" and don't give a damm about anyone's license.  And then
there's the distros out there that listen to those users and give them
the working distro as they see a market for it, and again, as a company,
justify to themselves that it must be ok to violate those kernel
developers rights because no one seems to be stopping them so far.

[side diversion, it's not the video drivers that really matter here
everyone, those are just so obvious.  It's the hundreds of other
blatantly infringing binary kernel modules out there that really matter.
The ones that control filesystems, cluster interconnects, disk arrays,
media codecs, and a whole host of custom hardware.  That's the real
problem that Linux faces now and will only get worse in the future.
It's not two stupid little video drivers, I could honestly care less
about them...][/quote]

[quote=Rik van Riel]Maybe we should just educate users and teach them to
avoid crazy unsupportable configurations and simply buy
the hardware that has open drivers available?

In the laptop space, I already try to avoid everything
non-Centrino, because chances are a closed source video
or network driver would be needed with something else.

Why would users buy a piece of hardware that needs a binary
only driver that's unsupportable, when they can buy a similar
piece of hardware that has a driver that's upstream and is
supported by every single Linux distribution out there?

Hardware companies: can you afford to miss out on the stability
and performance improvements that merging a driver upstream tends
to get?

Can you afford to miss out when your competitors are getting these
benefits?[/quote]

### Post by ago on 2006-12-19
Jono Bacon posted an article about his view on the decision to include closed drivers by default. [http://www.jonobacon.org/?p=843](http://www.jonobacon.org/?p=843)

As I commented on his blog I believe that the title and the full article is completely misleading. The choice is NOT Features vs. Freedom, but 1 click installation vs. 0 click installation.

You can still provide all the features within 1 click reach, and still compete with OSX on any other OS on eye candy and what not. Choosing freedom does not mean giving up features, it means letting people CHOOSE whether they want such non-free features BEFORE they get installed on THEIR machines. Nobody asks for the non-free driver to be difficult/impossible to install, just not to be installed by default whenever a FUNCTIONAL (even if less featurefull) free alternative is available. 

Is moving eye-candy 1 click away on *some* machines so unacceptable to be worth compromising freedom? This is the real question.

### Post by kanem on 2006-12-19
> **pay said:**
> I don't care if software is open source or not. I only care if the software is competent.
So, I take it that you use Ubuntu because you feel it is technically superior to Windows or Mac? If that's the case I'd like to point out that it is the open source nature of this operating system that allowed it to get technically superior. Linux would have gotten *nowhere* if it wasn't open. Same with Firefox or any of your other favourite Linux apps.

I've been seeing a lot of shortsightedness in these recent debates about graphics drivers. People seem to have the attitude of "Well, we've got this great Free operating system but it just needs a few more things. Who cares if we add a few closed source components?" Think about what that attitude would have gotten this operating system 20 or even 10 years ago; a dead end. No Linux, no Debian and certainly no Ubuntu.  Now think about what that present attitude will result in 10 or 20 years from now; a stale, slow moving system that has too many proprietary components to  allow devs to innovate.

Every extra piece of proprietary code that goes into this operating system is another piece that can't be improved by us in the future. It also makes it harder to improve the rest of the system that has to work with that closed off piece of software. But no one seems to think about the future.  "I want wobbly windows now" they say, forgetting that it was the forking of Xfree into the completely open Xorg that allowed developers to create those desktop effects. If *everything* about this operating system is going to improve it has to stay open so that the same genius that brought us Compiz and Beryl will be able to continue.

### Post by mushroom on 2006-12-19
The reason there shouldn't be a one-click installation instead of a zero-click installation is because no one wants a page displaying "Proprietary drivers are evil. Don't give in. If you click 'Yes' to this dialog, then you are evil. Free software free software free software free software." Functionality first, people. Compositing window managers open up a whole new way of displaying information to the user and in a more efficient way. Right now it provides usability enhancements with some mindless eye candy, but in the future it will accelerate every aspect of the desktop; video, games, you name it. OS X's had this functionality since Jaguar (2002!), and Vista has it as well. There are times when you have to set aside making every aspect of the OS conform to an ideology and think about what works. This will not create any slippery slopes or domino effects, we will not see a dystopian future full of robots whose only purpose is to oppress us with proprietary software. It's just a driver; one of the few pieces of software where it's excusable to be proprietary. This will not cease any efforts to create free drivers. The choice is there, you can very ably use the free drivers. For new users and users who don't like to get their hands dirty, proprietary drivers by default are necessary at this point.

### Post by DoctorMO on 2006-12-19
Yea people here that argue for binary drivers by default are blind.

1) You are not going to get help from devs, if it breaks tough luck go away.
2) If a new user has nvidia drivers by default and the driver breaks with a new version of X11 he will not think it's a problem with the driver, and when we tell them to go away because it's his driver he will not understand who to complain at.
3) People with ATI and Intel cards will have the nvidia driver installed, this does not make sense

> 2.1.1 Rights. Customer may install and use one copy of the SOFTWARE on a single computer, and except for making one back-up copy of the Software, may not otherwise copy the SOFTWARE. This LICENSE of SOFTWARE may not be shared or used concurrently on different computers.

Which is a problem

> 2.1.2 Linux/FreeBSD Exception. Notwithstanding the foregoing terms of Section 2.1.1, SOFTWARE designed exclusively for use on the Linux or FreeBSD operating systems, or other operating systems derived from the source code to these operating systems, may be copied and redistributed, provided that the binary files thereof are not modified in any way (except for unzipping of compressed files).

### Post by mushroom on 2006-12-19
> Well, apparently you are going to see this "boring" page anyway, [https://blueprints.launchpad.net/dis...iver-education](https://blueprints.launchpad.net/dis...iver-education)
 
 The problem is that they will first install the drivers, then show you the page, as opposed to do the opposite. So you see, one less reason to go for pre-installation vs easy-installation.

A pop-up balloon will be presented to the user upon boot, with more information should he or she be interested. This is not the same as presenting a dialog during installation to the user with information as to why proprietary drivers are perceived as bad. One is an irritating click-through. The other is a quickly and easily dismissed, non-intrusive warning. I would much prefer the latter.

Also, NVidia is a pretty respectable company, and I've found that its driver is of high quality. It performs as advertised, and I've never witnessed a kernel panic or anything of the sort while using it. The quality of the software shouldn't be in question, at least, not when pertaining to NVidia. ATI on the other hand...

No, I don't see a problem with making it "too" easy. You're acting as if using software that makes your hardware function properly is a disadvantage. You're also acting as if companies  are evil if they produce proprietary drivers for Linux. The drivers are proprietary because revealing the source would reveal trade secrets, and for companies like ATI and NVidia, that's not exactly an option. It's not a matter of preference, it's a matter of your hardware not functioning correctly without it. A lower level of support has been noted many times, and should be noted in the pop-up balloon upon first boot, but I don't really see it as a huge problem. If the company producing the driver is worth anything, it will patch the driver as soon as possible and Ubuntu will have it in the repos soon afterward.

Also, please construct your arguments in a coherent manner. I had to read your post five times, and I'm still not sure I understand it. Proper grammar may help.

> On top of it you are shooting in the foot of all the developers that right now are woring on OSS alternatives, "thanks for your work but we'd better use proprietary driver".

Oh please. If someone quits developing free drivers just because there are proprietary drivers available, then maybe he or she didn't really believe in free software in the first place. Once Nouveau performs as well as NVidia's driver, I'm all aboard. Right now, it doesn't even work. The free ATI drivers are incredibly outdated, which is doubly bad considering FGLRX sucks. Concerning hardware, I want function first, freedom second.

### Post by ago on 2006-12-20
> **steven8 said:**
> Argh!  Okay, we have determined that pretty much everybody values some sort of freedom.  Cool.  Quite understandable.  We have also established that the people who value, shall we call it "Freedom A", do so at the expense of those who value "Freedom B", while those who value "Freedom B", do so at the expense of those who value "Freedom A", and so on, and so on, and so on, and so on. . ."Can you hear me now?". . ."Can you hear me now?". . .ad infinitum(sp?). . .okay ad nauseum.  Everyone wishes to restrict the others with their own brand of "Freedom", thus claiming to be 'freeing' everyone.

And remember. . .I have the freedom to feel this way. :-)

And you are missing the point completely, together with many others here. The point is not Features VS Freedom. I have said it, I have repeated it, and people keeps falling into the same faulty argument. The point is about being able to CHOOSE.

There are 2 types of freedom involved here, 

1) the use of free software i.e. sacrificing features for freedom when open alternatives are not on par with closed ones 
2) the ability to choose whether to use free software or to have more features. 

People keep confusing the two, and that is misleading at best. In fact the two are antithetic. Forcing free software (1) and/or making it difficult to install closed solutions would violate (2) just as much as forcing closed solutions and/or making it difficult to uninstall them. 

There is nothing wrong with having binary drivers available and even easy to install (1), what is very wrong is to install them without even giving users the chance to make an INFORMED DECISION about it (2). The fact that point (1) is violated is not the issue, the issue is that point (2) is violated. And what exactly is the need to violate point (2)? Are we afraid about users making INFORMED DECISIONS? Why? Is 1 click installation going to screw the competitiveness of ubuntu when compared to 0 click installation? I do not think so. And remember that Ubuntu did decide to inform the users, they will just not make them choose on the base of such information. Does anybody else see the problem here? They are preaching against closed solutions but they take the liberty to choose for (from) the user to install the solutions they preached against.  

The only justifications for such a shortsighted choice so far have been arguments about features vs freedom. Those justifications do NOT explain why (2) has been violated and are completely pointless. What Mark and the others should explain is why Ubuntu does not want users to make INFORMED DECISIONS and that has absolutely nothing to do with features.

---

If I could put a binary driver that would make my Bluetooth work or one that would make my special function buttons work and use Ubuntu I would or even a proprietary tool that allows me the same configuration options as Windows does on the tablet. If I had free drivers for all of my hardware and free software for configuration I would be one happy geek. 

The problem at hand is lack of free drivers for certain hardware that provide the same functionality, as I mentioned before the NV driver couldn't even render my 2D desktop properly. Just because I want wobbly windows is hardly a reason for me to switch from an excellent OS to a crappy one. 

As far as the topic goes I think the most sensible thing would be to ask the user. If the setup finds a whitelisted card it should ask a question whether compositing should be enabled, with a brief explanation and a link to more in depth info (possibly only on the Desktop CD). If the user decides that compositing should be enabled the setup then can install the drivers, if free drivers are available that will do compositing then that's what's going to be installed and there is no need for more interaction. However if the driver to be installed is binary the user must then be presented with a choice and explanation as he/she does with compositing. It can be expanded to any proprietary software that setup might install by default. Of course it should all be on the install CD.

This should please both proponents of FREE software and the Just Works people. Neither will have to manually remove/install something after the initial setup is done.

### Post by prizrak on 2006-12-20
> **ago said:**
> And you are missing the point completely, together with many others here. The point is not Features VS Freedom. I have said it, I have repeated it, and people keeps falling into the same faulty argument. The point is about being able to CHOOSE.

There are 2 types of freedom involved here, 

1) the use of free software i.e. sacrificing features for freedom when open alternatives are not on par with closed ones 
2) the ability to choose whether to use free software or to have more features. 

People keep confusing the two, and that is misleading at best. In fact the two are antithetic. Forcing free software (1) and/or making it difficult to install closed solutions would violate (2) just as much as forcing closed solutions and/or making it difficult to uninstall them. 

There is nothing wrong with having binary drivers available and even easy to install (1), what is very wrong is to install them without even giving users the chance to make an INFORMED DECISION about it (2). The fact that point (1) is violated is not the issue, the issue is that point (2) is violated. And what exactly is the need to violate point (2)? Are we afraid about users making INFORMED DECISIONS? Why? Is 1 click installation going to screw the competitiveness of ubuntu when compared to 0 click installation? I do not think so. And remember that Ubuntu did decide to inform the users, they will just not make them choose on the base of such information. Does anybody else see the problem here? They are preaching against closed solutions but they take the liberty to choose for (from) the user to install the solutions they preached against.  

The only justifications for such a shortsighted choice so far have been arguments about features vs freedom. Those justifications do NOT explain why (2) has been violated and are completely pointless. What Mark and the others should explain is why Ubuntu does not want users to make INFORMED DECISIONS and that has absolutely nothing to do with features.

How does one default take away your freedom but another default does not? If the default driver is free, the user can easily install a non-free one and remove the free one. If the default driver is not free, the user can easily install a free one and remove the non-free one. Where is the limit in choice here? Would you say that the window list being on the top panel is stopping a user from making an informed decision? I for one don't like it up there and always move it down. I'm not about to bitch at Canonical for that default, perhaps it works for more people than the other way. 

This is basically the age old argument of defaults. If Windows defaults to IE is it anti-competitive or is it OK because no one is stopping you from setting Firefox as default? 

Basically I don't see how freedom is violated by having one driver as a default over another. No OS can be everything to everyone and according to forum polls about 90% of people install proprietary drivers so Canonical is trying to set the defaults that would work for them. 

As matthew said (and many others including me) if you have a problem with binary closed source drivers don't use the hardware that is closed. The best thing you can do to make a difference is vote with your wallet, support manufacturers that open up their specs and don't buy from those who don't. Be vocal about it, make sure the manufacturer knows why people aren't buying their products. Don't buy closed hardware and think you are making a difference by using free drivers, nVidia doesn't give a damn what you do after you paid for the product, you can nail it to the wall and they still won't care.

### Post by ago on 2006-12-20
> This is basically the age old argument of defaults. If Windows defaults to IE is it anti-competitive or is it OK because no one is stopping you from setting Firefox as default?
Having something in by default does mean PUSHING that over the alternatives. 

When windows installs something by default it is an issue because it creates a DE FACTO STANDARD. The claims that people are free to choose otherwise is bulloks, because people that diverge from what they found out of the box are a minority, and minorities do not set the standards. Ubuntu, covers the majority of the Linux desktop market, and what choices it makes by default are very important within the Linux world. 

It is very different giving user a working box (with free driver) and asking if they want extra features in exchange for freedom, or to give them a working box (with closed drivers) and ask them to reduce the feautures for freedom. You will observe a lot of resilience: many people that would not install closed drivers in the first case would not uninstall them in the second case. 

If you want to be neutral give them the option to choose BEFOREHAND after having stated pros and cons (i.e. during installation). If you want to push open software preinstall it and then give the option to replace it later. If you want to push closed software, preinstall it and give the option to replace it later. So no, the default choice is NOT irrelevant, because it exploits people natural laziness and risk-aversion.

Even worse, uninstalling closed solutions is not as trivial as you claim. Of course you can use apt/synaptic but that is not for average joe and it is not the same as clicking "yes, screw freedom" in the initial balloon to install closed software. So there you have another asymmetry.

The information page shown ex-post IMO makes things even worse, it is like saying: "you have been a bad boy, but here are the sweeties anyway".

### Post by deanlinkous on 2006-12-20
Free software is not some weird idea. The four freedoms are very basic common ideas that are ethical and for the most part I would think that most everyone would agree that they are good ideas.

I mean if you apply the four freedoms to other products you will see that it is something you believe in and do not even think twice about it.

(weird example)
I purchased a pasta strainer the other day - I used it as a cat litter scooper. I didn't like the tiny holes so I modified it. My neighbor seen it and asked about it so I went to the store and purchased the product and did the same for them. That neighbor improved it and showed me so I implemented the same changes on mine.
All that was the four freedoms in action just applied to a different product.

The four freedoms, not verbatim since I am using them to show how we all believe in them when it comes to other products.
1 any purpose
2 study andadapt it 
3 redistribute 
4 freedom to improve and release your improvements

I bought a car, I can use it for any purpose. I am not limited to certain roads. I can take it off road. I can fill it full of water and sardines. I can use it for any purpose - I do not sign a 'certain use only' clause when I purchase it.

I can study my car. I can adapt it to my needs. I do this VERY thing. I have some tweaks and changes I do to my cars.

I can tell/show others how to do the same to their car and copy what I have done.

Others can improve on my ideas and release them as well.

Every one of us can think of other things like these examples. From lamp oil to condition vinyl, to foam placed on the hard drive rail to stop resonation, to all the uses for duct tape....the list is never ending. I dare say a day goes by that those *freedoms* or abilities do not come into play somehow. 

Why accept those freedoms (abilities) for every product yet not even think twice when it comes to throwing them away on one of the most important products that exist. 

You buy your turbo charger from greedy, yet if it needs a little tweak, a small change, a different hole in a certain place you what..... you change it right? Why are you willing to use closed drivers that take away your fair use of them. They tell you what you can and cannot do and you accept that because they throw you a bone with that cool xrender function and you call that choice.

---

### Post by aysiu on 2006-12-20
Mark Shuttleworth explained this on his website: > Ubuntu has included firmware, and used proprietary drivers since its inception. That&#8217;s always been a slightly uncomfortable proposition, as Mako observed, but it&#8217;s been true since the Warty Warthog. Even Debian goes some way along that road with its inclusion of firmware and other non-free bits, suspending belief in the DFSG in this particular case. We discussed this at length in one of our earliest summits and settled on the hardware enablement vs apps boundary. All right? So there have always been non-free bits, and the distinction/boundary is hardware versus applications.

Read more here if you care to find out what's really going on and not just be argumentative about slippery slopes and such:
[http://www.markshuttleworth.com/archives/84](http://www.markshuttleworth.com/archives/84)

### Post by aysiu on 2006-12-20
Well, I think Mark makes it clear where it stops--hardware.

It has always been hardware, since Warty, and it continues to be hardware now.

Let's just say, though, we agree with the whole "slippery slope" idea. Honestly, who cares where it stops? If it bothers you that much, you can use Debian or something else. And if it ever gets so bad that Ubuntu becomes Linspire, there were always be other Freer alternatives. You're never trapped using Ubuntu. There's no vendor lock-in. The beauty of Linux is choice. You don't like this distribution, you use another. You don't like that distribution, you can fork it and make your own.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-12-20
Honestly, one of the things I love about Linux is that you **don't** have to complain about such things once they've already been decided.

If you're on Windows, and Microsoft makes some crummy decision you don't agree with... well, tough luck. If you're on Mac OS X, and Apple makes some crummy decision you don't agree with... also, tough luck.

But if you're using Ubuntu, and Mark Shuttleworth makes a crummy decision you don't agree with, well, you're in luck! You can use all the same applications you were using before, the same desktop environment, the same themes... on another distribution! You can switch to Gentoo, LFS, Debian, Slackware... whatever suits your fancy. You can take the Ubuntu source code and make your own version--the opposite of Linux Mint. You can use GnuBuntu when it comes out:
[http://www.ubuntux.org/gnubuntu-a-completely-free-ubuntu](http://www.ubuntux.org/gnubuntu-a-completely-free-ubuntu)

### Post by Artemis3 on 2006-12-21
Ok this merging of threads must be an act of evilness, who is going to read all 69 pages?

Anyways, in the merged thread i see some people bashing harshly others who try to question recent decisions. And i see uninformed statements, especially concerning free software, open source, blobs, ubuntu, etc. Its so easy to call others with names than to stop and _read_ a little. These people are quick to defend what they believe, but don't give a minute or two reading about the arguments being exposed.

I'll try to summarize and repeat what i have said elsewhere where i think some people have missed some information.

The definition of Free Software. Free software is not about not paying, it is about freedom. You can still pay or ask to be paid to fix or make things; what you can't is to make others depend on you or anyone else. This is the key point with free software: You will never be tied to anyone.

Ubuntu is not free software. We agree. Since Warty, there have been ndiswrapper blobs, without them, said hardware would not work at all. This is not true with the 3d cards. The 3d cards do have working drivers, for 2d operations, which people needing a desktop to work can use. Open 3d cards do exist, offered by Intel and Matrox among others. Also ATI is no more, what they though in the past doesn't matter anymore, AMD is now in charge and they have stated already that they believe in being open and plan to document the instruction set for their coming GPUs, some which will be included in multicore units along with the CPU. In months, nVidia will be strangling against the wall alone in their selfishness. The trade secret excuse is a myth, most likely they could be using patented technologies without permission, another problem entirely.

Then, when we try to suggest an option at install time, it doesn't really mean a preaching. It could be simple like: Choose driver: nv (no 3d but free) nvidia (3d but propietary). A reasonable default would be the free one. I don't understand what is wrong with asking a simple question. Aren't you mocking us about freedom? How about freedom to choose? Ok, [we can go to another Distro](http://www.gnewsense.org/), its probably the best thing to do. But what is this nosense that a single click is "too hard for users"? Can't you see the hypocrisy ubuntu is doing? Do you think its user friendly to enable mp3 audio and mp4 video playback? It is certainly more than a single click; you have to add repositories and install a bunch of packages. Yes, [they can also go to another distro](http://lt.k1011.nutime.de/about.html)... But don't say you default to blobs because its easier when you make hell to people simply wanting to play their music or watch their videos.

Also what is this utter nonsense of us forbiding anyone to install blobs and propietary software? You can do it to ututo, and to gnewsense, and with ubuntu and any other distro and even non linux OSes (yes, free software operating systems exist beyond linux, and you don't need hurd). But defaulting the use of them is another matter. I will put you an example: How about defaulting opera instead of firefox? Thats the equivalent of defaulting nvidia vs nv. How about defaulting Internet Explorer (it runs under wine) then? Thats the equivalent of using a windows driver via ndiswrapper to make your wifi card work.

On top of that, some love to insult the free software community, and then quickly defend the rights of the corporations. But who you gave your money to? Why you don't demand THEM to open the specs so the community can make decent drivers? Why don't you help the reverse engineering heroes who make things work when nobody else could? If you don't code you can always pay someone to work or donate to a group.

Just because you never need the blueprints doesn't mean the day you need to hire a plumber to do a major work won't be needing them. Else someone would have to reverse engineer your house... What if your new house was a black box with a key only the landowner has? And you have to use your landowner plumbing with his own conditions?

What is the reason you started to use Ubuntu in the first place?, why don't you keep using your windows for all your gaming needs? What is wrong with dual booting? Did anybody forced you to wipe the other partition? You came to free software because of security, and stability right? Then what is the point of installing blobs that makes you lose exactly that?

nVidia binaries have already caused lockups and buffer overflows issues, does it sounds like some other os? And guess what, no one can fix it, it is a black box, like the other OS is.
You have amd/ia64? not working? too bad, go pledge to nVidia. You depend again, you have lost your freedom. This you might not care, but don't blame others who do.

Ubuntu is the distro "that just works" to make your desktop a cube, but can't play a stupid mp3. Don't you see the double standard here?

In the long run, this is not even true either. Short of Canonical actually signing the NDA most closed hardware manufacturers offer to 3rd parties wanting to make a driver (with very limited, if any, rights to redistribution) it is impossible to maintain and support what you can't touch or see. The day something changes, you have to wait for that corporation to release their update, if ever. Just like you have to wait for Internet Explorer bugs to get fixed, and like IE, wait for new ones to explode in your face.

Now i will cite again OpenBSD. OpenBSD, is, fyi, another free software operating system that fits the Free Software definition and complies with the 4 liberties. It is not linux and not gnu. But you can add gnu apps as well as propietary ones, if thats your intent, but its YOUR choice, not theirs. And they don't need to make it easy for you, just like the linux kernel developers are NOT making it easy for binary drivers to creep in. Linux IS GPLed, never forget that.

> **"http://www.openbsd.org/lyrics.html"]OpenBSD emphasizes security. It also emphasizes openness. All the code is there for all to see. Blobs are vendor-compiled binary drivers without any source code. Hardware makers like them because they obscure the details of how to make their hardware work. They hide bugs and workarounds for bugs. Newer versions of blobs can weaken support for older hardware and motivate people to buy new hardware.

Blobs are expedient. Many other open source operating systems cheerfully incorporate them said:**
> [*] Blobs can be 'de-supported' by vendors at any time.
    [*] Blobs cannot be supported by developers.
    [*] Blobs cannot be fixed by developers.
    [*] Blobs cannot be improved.
    [*] Blobs cannot be audited.
    [*] Blobs are specific to an architecture, thus less portable.
    [*] Blobs are quite often massively bloated.[/list]

This release, like every OpenBSD release, contains OpenBSD and its source code. It runs on a wide variety of hardware. It contains many new features and improvements. OpenBSD does attempt to convince vendors to release documentation, and often reverse-engineers around the need for blobs. OpenBSD remains blob-free. Anyone can look at it, assess it, improve it. If it breaks, it can be fixed.

### Post by steven8 on 2006-12-21
**[COLOR="Red"]**WARNING** Smart-Alek comment ahead:[/COLOR]**

From the OpenBSD website:

> Some other open source operating systems are commonly distributed as CD-ROM ISO images. This is not how OpenBSD is distributed. 
The OpenBSD project does not make the ISO images used to master the official CDs available for download. The reason is simply that we would like you to buy the CD sets to help fund ongoing OpenBSD development. **The official OpenBSD CD-ROM layout is copyright Theo de Raadt. Theo does not permit people to redistribute images of the official OpenBSD CDs.** As an incentive for people to buy the CD set, some extras are included in the package as well (artwork, stickers etc). 

But what if i want to take Theo's design and tweak it to my tastes?  Why do they not have someone create a design that is open to change?  That goes against the total freeness idea.

In other words, this can get very sticky very fast.

### Post by darkninja on 2006-12-21
> **steven8 said:**
> 
But what if i want to take Theo's design and tweak it to my tastes?  Why do they not have someone create a design that is open to change?  That goes against the total freeness idea.

In other words, this can get very sticky very fast.

Trademark/Copyright != License

You're also technically not allowed to use the Ubuntu, Debian or Linux names and logos without permission from their respective copyright owners.

I can't make my own OS kernel and name it Linux because "Linux" is a trademark of Linus Torvalds. However you are still free to fork the Linux source and then call it whatever the hell you want :p Same with Ubuntu. 

That said, Ubuntu and most other FOSS related projects are very fair when it comes to reusing their IP.

Mozilla recently lashed out at distros using their trademark on unofficial Firefox builds, causing the entire IceWeasel incident.

### Post by darkninja on 2006-12-21
I often think that it's in fact those who are in favour of installing the binary drivers who are the "Idealists".

As it stands, Linux desktop market share would be around 2% at maximum. Let's just **unrealistically** assume that enabling closed source drivers by default would bring in enough new users to *double* the size of our marketshare.

Will that cause Nvidia/ATi to make better drivers or open their specs? Of course not, we'd still remain a tiny speck on their radar. The gaming market they mostly cater for are firmly glued to Windows. Once we use closed-source drivers by default, there will be no going back. Other commercial distros will follow. The demand for free drivers will be diminished.

Linux is a decent alternative for many users however the vast majority are satisfied with XP and don't have reason or desire to care about this little OS war. Their next OS will be whatever is sold with their PC.

We can't and don't need to defeat Vista **right now**. Trying to beat Windows at gaming and 3D performance is suicide. Eventually we'll win, no need to rush it by doing stupid things might come back to bite us later. It's just a matter of time.

Face it, Joe User only plays a tiny part in the success of Linux. Steve Developer on the other hand, is absolutely essential for the survival of Linux. Moves that put the **wants of the user** ahead of the **needs of developers** are short-sighted and ultimately endanger the future of Linux.

The current situation (user installed binaries) is possibly the best compromise between functionality and necessary politics.

---

### Post by prizrak on 2006-12-21
> **steven8 said:**
> I'm using SwiftFox in Kubuntu.  Go figger!! :-)

SwiftFox has a very restrictive license. Copyright by the way is what keeps Linux free, it is the copyright that allows for GPL to be legally enforced. 

Artemis,
As stated before a million times, including binary drivers from nVidia/AMD is LEGAL there is no question whatsoever about it. MP3, w32codecs, libdvdcs, etc... are legally questionable in some countries they are legal in others they are not. According to a talk Mark gave on Feisty Ubuntu will actually enable things like MP3 playback in countries where that patent is not recognized, at the very least there is work that is being done in that direction.

When did AMD ever say they were going to open source the drivers? Last I heard was AMD saying quite the opposite, when the community was wondering about it AMD reps said that ATI will be allowed to do things as they see fit and AMD will not interfere with them. If AMD does open source the driver so will nVidia there won't be much reason for them to stay closed. Even if they don't we will still have powerful 3D card with open drivers so it doesn't really matter, hardware wise Radeons and GeForces are pretty much on the level. 

nVidia driver causes issues? That is news to me, never had a problem with them under Ubuntu not even the beta driver. Intel's open source driver however gave me a crapload of headache. Also guess what, I still had to wait for someone to fix it (and as of Edgy it wasn't). 

I don't see a problem with a default being proprietary vs free. There really isn't any, for one simple reason. If you care about it you can remove/install it. This default is making it easy for the 85% of people who do install the binary driver versus making it easy for the 15% of people who are all about free software. You see where the majority is don't you? 

I do agree that asking is the best possible solution, this way Ubuntu can come with all the binary drivers out of the box and make the install much better. It would also be nice if it could configure NDISwrapper with the Windows driver for those cards that are not supported. Of course all should be presented to the user for choice. 

As far as dual booting, well we don't want to dual boot and sure I can game on Windows or a console but I can't get wobbly Windows and a spinning cube, think what you want but I like amusing effects.


> Once we use closed-source drivers by default, there will be no going back. Other commercial distros will follow. 
Might be news to you but other commercial distros already install binary drivers as the default. According to the spec page one of the reasons Ubuntu is doing it, is because SLED, Mandriva and RedHat do. I know for a fact that SLED comes with Compositing on out of the box so they definetly aren't using nv. 


> The demand for free drivers will be diminished.
Complete and utter BS and you know it. If that was the case we wouldn't have the NV driver at all, there wouldn't be the noveau(sp?) project that is trying to do open source 3D. In fact if having a closed source alternative meant low demand for open source to the point where people would stop coding you wouldn't be on Ubuntu right now. Both XP and OS X are good and stable enough for 95% of people out there. 

> The current situation (user installed binaries) is possibly the best compromise between functionality and necessary politics.

### Post by prizrak on 2006-12-21
> **deanlinkous said:**
> Sure it does. The kernel even says it is tainted. No a copyright holder(s) takes exception ot it and has the ability to send it to court. While it may in the end proven to be legal it is not currently clear. It depends on how you see it. Anything that uses the kernel IMO is "linked" to the kernel and forms a derivative product of that amalgamation.  
The GPL is clear on that, it says that any output that a GPL'ed program creates is not covered by the license. The nVidia driver simply calls kernel functions it is not a kernel module. Linus touched on that, he said that banning binary from the kernel will make people do what nVidia does and run from userspace. The driver is actually an X module and uses system calls just like X does. Not sure what X people think about it but it really has no bearing on the kernel. 

>  
feel free to google
Who knows what the next version does? Might even have a nice rootkit just for fun. The problem is - who knows what problems the next version causes?

If you make an argument you have to back it up otherwise it's just talk. I agree that we don't know what's comming in the next version but we don't know that in free software either. Xorg 7.1 issue was resolved pretty quickly. I have stated before that I believe free software to be technologically better (taking the morality out of this). 
 
> 
But certainly just the ability to do so is desirable? 

Never said otherwise, my only point here was that it is not as important to the end user as you seem to think.

> 
What democracy dude?

That's right we have a benevolent dictator, generally speaking defaults are created for the majority of users not minority. 

> 
The reason those distros include it is because they ARE commercial. If Ubuntu idoes the same and acts the same well....walks,talks like a duck...
[\QUOTE]
Like I said that is an opinion issue. Lets agree to disagree.
[QUOTE]
 No they were talking about decreased demand for free software because of availability of proprietary software for  free software system. Not the same thing.
[\QUOTE]
That would be a point of view issue, I think it is the same thing.
Why in the world would there ever be less demand for free software? If there was an open driver that does 3D for nVidia cards at least enough to run compositing if not games I wouldn't be using the binary. Nouveau project was started fairly recently despite nVidia drivers being available for a while. 

[QUOTE]
So now you are saying what HE was talking about...uh ok.  I guess it depends on how you define neutral.

[QUOTE=http://wordnet.princeton.edu/perl/webwn?s=neutral]
S: (adj) neutral (not supporting or favoring either side in a war, dispute, or contest)
[/QUOTE]
So letting a user decide would be as neutral as you can possibly get. 

> Red Hat and Novell do not ship binary drivers only the smaller compaines do that
[QUOTE=http://www.novell.com/products/desktop/features.html] # Enhanced 3-D Accelerated Graphics
Novell added graphics capabilities to the desktop to help your users achieve new levels of productivity. Through Novell contributions (i.e., Xgl and 'Compiz') to the open source graphics subsystem, users get the latest functionality in SUSE Linux Enterprise Desktop. Key features include:

    * Application tiling, which provides a snapshot of all open applications
    * Alt tabbing, which includes thumbnail views of open applications
    * Three-dimensional desktop, which provides additional space and more efficient use of your desktop
[/QUOTE]
Would not be possible without binary drivers on nVidia cards.

I was actually rereading the spec and this discussion seems very out of proportion at this point. 
[QUOTE=Ubuntu Wiki]Open Source Video Drivers

    *

      The opensource 'nv' driver does not support 3D acceleration or Composite, but will be installed by default, and used on the following use cases:
          o

            NVIDIA users with current video boards (supported via the 'nvidia' driver) will have the chance to use this driver as an alternative to the proprietary driver
          o

            NVIDIA users with legacy video boards (supported via the 'nvidia_legacy' driver) will have the 'nv' driver enabled by default, due to limited support of the legacy proprietary driver
    *

      The 'nv' open source has no Composite or 3D acceleration support, and thus, the experimental Nouveau 2D/3D driver will be offered as a third alternative for users with NVIDIA boards. This driver is currently considered under development, and will be offered on a best-effort basis. It is considered functional for various use cases, though. The Nouveau driver *will* also be considered as an alternative as default NVIDIA driver for Feisty+1, in case it is stable enough in six months from now
    *

      The Open Source 'ATI' driver will be enabled by default for any ati video board it supports (this excludes the newest X1300-X1950, but all others should work, 2D and 3D, except for possible bugs or required special options)
[/QUOTE]
Things that jump out:
1) Both drivers are installed by default you get to choose what to use. 
2) Radeons will still get the open driver aside from those that are just plain not supported by it. 
3) The Nouveau driver will be offered on best-effort basis, so it's really up to the Nouveau devs at this point whether we get binary or free. 
4) If Nouveau is stable and functional in time for Feisty+1 it will be used in place of the binary. 

So to recap, some of the newer nVidia cards will get the binary driver. The newest Radeons too (I think I'm not sure if they are supported by anything but VESA actually) and only because the free driver doesn't even work on them (that's what I gather from the spec). The rest will be nice and free and it's a temporary short term patch until a free 3D driver comes out.

### Post by Kratos on 2006-12-21
Let me start by acknowledging the nobility of the cause in making Linux 100% open source. It's a way of "sticking it to the man" and showing every other company that an operating system can stand on it's own feet and be able to allow anyone to look under the hood. 

However, I can also appreciate the idea of making Ubuntu easier to use for those less-experienced users. True, we may be thinking in the same spectrum as Microsoft; scrabbling for market share with pretty colors. However, if we can 'convert' more people, there may be more talented people who would be willing to contribute to free alternatives. It's a matter of self-preservation, really. Linux and OSS in general is software by the user for the user. As such, the more users, the more chances of creating a better system. 

So, if the coders want to include binary drivers in the default install, so be it. If it gets more able-minded people willing to learn and contribute to our cause, I see no problem. If they decide to give us the option, I'll gladly use the provided binary drivers and have faith in both the Ubuntu developement team and the nVidia developement team. I have no reason to distrust either of them.

---

### Post by ago on 2006-12-21
> **Kratos said:**
> Let me start by acknowledging the nobility of the cause in making Linux 100% open source. It's a way of "sticking it to the man" and showing every other company that an operating system can stand on it's own feet and be able to allow anyone to look under the hood. 

However, I can also appreciate the idea of making Ubuntu easier to use for those less-experienced users.

You could have both by being INFORMED and ASKED during installation or after installation whether you want to have functionality in exchange for freedom. Moving 3D functionality 1 click away does not make any difference whatsoever to the ease of use and market share. The ease of use is no justification. What is pre-installed by default is a statement, it is the alternative your distro sees as "better", it is the technology your distro will push (based on people laziness and risk aversion to try non-default alternatives). And Ubuntu will push closed drivers, the ex-post balloon does not change that. On top of it preinstalling without asking when the object is something many people have strong objections against is never a nice attitude. There is a big difference between preinstalling something people do not need and preinstalling something people do not want.

### Post by ago on 2006-12-21
I wrote this petition

> 
Dear Mark, dear Ubuntu developers,

We the undersigned petition you to reconsider your decision of including binary drivers by default in the next Ubuntu release. We believe, as you do, that it should be as easy as possible for end users to enable extra functionality via closed software when necessary and if they so wish, but only after an explicit acknowledgement and only after being informed about the issues at stake and possible alternatives. Users can be given the option to install closed drivers/software during installation or soon after installation or when an application that may require such software is run for the first time. It is a small change, but one that will avoid dividing the community on an issue that many hold dear, without sacrificing ease of use and product appeal. We therefore encourage you to opt for easy-installation as opposed to pre-installation. 

Many thanks for your attention,




EDIT: I decided to follow Givre' suggestion and make the petition available on the wiki (my main reservations were about people needing to be registered in order to sign). 

This is the page: [https://wiki.ubuntu.com/NoBlobsByDefaultPetition](https://wiki.ubuntu.com/NoBlobsByDefaultPetition) please consider signing the petition and making others know about it.

---

 install

 text-install

 genuine

 console

 internetkiosk

 internetkiosknx

 debug

 memtest[/quote]

        * - This distribution contains libdvdcss look here if you live in the U.S. !.
        * - This distribution contains proprietary and non-GPL softwares too (like from NVIDIA, ATI, Google, etc). To run SabayonLinux without Proprietary drivers, just use "noproprietary" boot flag.
        * - Reiser4 is disabled on the Installer (to enabled it just add "reiser4" to your boot options) - USE AT YOUR OWN RISK!

The best proprietary software can never be fixed, audited, improved or studied the day you want to, the worst of free software can. This difference is what made things like Ubuntu possible.

[quote="Fabio Erculiani"]I want to clear out all your doubts about ATI and NVIDIA proprietary drivers inclusion.
Starting from version 3.1, we do not ship those binary blobs anymore but, during the Live boot, they are runtime linked (I call this: runtime linking) and the final kernel modules (nvidia.ko and fglrx.ko) are generated back.

If you do not agree with this default behavior,  you only need to add "noproprietary" to the boot parameters.[/quote]

Don't live in the illusion that all people think like you. [Blobs](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_blob) are a serious problem.

### Post by prizrak on 2006-12-22
> Mozilla can be fixed by anyone, you can't hide anything as you do have the source. Where is your proof the nVidia driver is safe and secure? Have you learn anything of IE? The nVidia driver is a black box. For all we care, it might even be a trojan or have time bombs.
Sure it can be fixed by anyone but is it? You don't have to convicne me that free software is better than proprietary. If you paid any attention to my post you would see that I agree with you. However the ability to fix a hole doesn't mean it's actually fixed, just as me being able to drive 120mph doesn't mean that I necessarily will. In the end when your system gets rooted it doesn't really matter where the hole was and if it could have been fixed by anyone. 
> No it is not. Free and secure by default is much better than proprietary and unsafe. You live in the illusion that Free Software and Proprietary can be exactly the same, no they cannot. The best proprietary software can never be fixed, audited, improved or studied the day you want to, the worst of free software can. This difference is what made things like Ubuntu possible.
You are making a very faulty assumption that free software is inherently secure and proprietary isn't. Apache slammer showed us quite nicely that free software is just as full of holes, I won't even mention SSL issues that are so widely known they were in the Matrix movie. I do agree that for those who just leave their system alone a default free driver means security updates not so with binary. I still don't believe in choosing one over the other for the user let the user choose. 
> That doesn't mean you can't also add a boottime option, as you can see, sabayon does have both. Speaking of Sabayon:
That's a design decision and really doesn't matter either way IMO. Live CD should run free driver anyway since video does work with it in 2D. 
> Don't live in the illusion that all people think like you. Blobs are a serious problem.
I think you are under the illusion that I think that. I'm pragmatic I spent 1300 on a system that has nVidia card in it because of it's power I want to use that power so I will install a binary driver so long as there is no free alternative. 

As far as security goes the only real way towards security is allowing only known things to run and dissalowing everything else. SELinux is one mecahnism that will let you do that. Free or not nothing is ever 100%.

### Post by prizrak on 2006-12-25
> **Tomosaur said:**

I'm saying that everything is a black box to a certain extent to certain people. A car's engine is a black box to my mother, she couldn't tell an air intake from a radiator. The point I was trying to make is that if you say that proprietary closed drivers are inherently bad because you are trusting someone else and have no way to see what's going on is simply wrong. As a side point to the main one I was saying that nothing is truly black boxed because there are always ways of seeing what is going on inside. As far as the main topic of this thread goes I think it is evident that most people believe in letting the user choose what he/she wants to use as oppose to forcing them to use one and then alter the default config to his/hers taste.

### Post by mushroom on 2006-12-25
I'm just not sure why you would run Ubuntu and suddenly cause an uproar over some stupid userland program that can't even cause the system to crash. Ubuntu was never an ideologically pure OS, wi-fi firmware has always existed within it. In a discussion of free software in general, these concerns would be valid. This is not that discussion. Or at least, it wasn't, so I might as well add my thoughts on that.

For me, the most important feature of free software is the freedom to redistribute without restriction. I couldn't care less about seeing the source or even modifying it. One of the things that drew me to Ubuntu was not having to deal with licensing issues. I could install it on as many machines as I wanted and make copies of it to give to someone else. That's really all I care about concerning software. Proprietary drivers give me this freedom, or at least I think they do. If not, then Ubuntu's going to be in a mess of trouble.

Back to the main point, though. I'm not dismissing the notion of being bothered about the existance of proprietary drivers, but it makes no sense to raise concern over their inclusion in Ubuntu, a distro that has never adhered to exclusively free software. gNewSense, BLAG and Ututo are the only distros I can come up with off the top of my head that do, so go run one of them.

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-05
> **Lord Illidan said:**
> Sometimes I think that FSF guys must either be extremely pessimistic or else plain zealots.

You have that backwards. I am VERY optimistic about the future of Linux. It has finally reached the point of being included in any mainstream discussion about OSes, millions of boxen are about to be distributed through the OLPC project, governments and corporations around the world are switching Free/Open Source Software, Apple has switched their previously closed QuickTime format over to the more open MPEG-4 AVC standard, and the U.S. Supreme Court is currently hearing arguments that software should not be patentable. This all portends great things for the future of Linux and Free Software.

On the video driver front, Intel (which holds 40% of the graphics adapter market) has recently released Open drivers for their latest hardware and is actively contributing to the development of X.Org; Advanced Micro Devices, which is currently being quite friendly towards Free Software in their other endeavors, has recently purchased ATI (which has 27% of the market) and has hinted to a willingness to provide Open drivers. It is only NVidia (which clocks in at 20% of the graphics market) who seems to be holding a firm stance against opening up access to their hardware and withdrawing support for their 2D drivers. 

It is the Chicken Little doomsday attitude that Linux will not survive unless it starts playing by the closed, proprietary rules that is "pessimistic" and it is the proposed "endorsement" of NVidia's stance which is counterproductive to the Freedom in computing that has been the goal of the Free Software movement for the last quarter-century.

> **darkhatter said:**
> at the end of this day its Mark Shuttleworth's distro. He does what he wants with it,..

If what you say is true then the Ubuntu Philosophy is indeed relegated from the level of "lip service" to "outright deception" (I hope that it is not true).

---

### Post by prizrak on 2007-01-05
> **zetetic said:**
> You must be kidding or you don't know the difference between firmware and drivers.

It's true Debian also ships with firmware, but binary drivers are much worst than firmware (in terms of stability, security, privacy user's lock in, etc).

But Debian doesn't install binary drivers by default while Ubuntu will ship binary video drivers by default!

Besides Debian is commited to eliminate all firmware, if possible on the next stable release (pearhaps when lenny goes stable), while Ubuntu is not.

If you still think the right decision is binary drivers by default in Ubuntu, that's fine! It's your opinion and we must respect it.

But please don't try to through sand to our eyes. Don't compare apples with oranges.. Give us a break.

zetetic
If you read the discussion on LKLM where one of the devs created a patch that would not allow binary blobs in the kernel you will see that you are quite wrong about stability. nVidia driver is in userland btw and not kernel so it actually has very little effect on system stability. In fact in my experience the open source mplayer is the most unstable thing that can completely lock up X regardless of what driver is being used. 

As far as Debian getting rid of firmware by the next stable release goes. Well considering Debian releases about every 3 years that's quite a ways to go. Also Ubuntu is based on Debian and if Debian gets rid of the firmware in a way that doesn't compromise functionality then Ubuntu will have it as well. 

Yes a driver is different from a piece of firmware but both are closed source in this case and affect the system quite a bit. Firmware actually more so as it sits in the kernel as opposed to being a userland program that simply talks to it.

### Post by prizrak on 2007-01-05
> **saulgoode said:**
> 
On the video driver front, Intel (which holds 40% of the graphics adapter market) has recently released Open drivers for their latest hardware and is actively contributing to the development of X.Org; Advanced Micro Devices, which is currently being quite friendly towards Free Software in their other endeavors, has recently purchased ATI (which has 27% of the market) and has hinted to a willingness to provide Open drivers. It is only NVidia (which clocks in at 20% of the graphics market) who seems to be holding a firm stance against opening up access to their hardware and withdrawing support for their 2D drivers. 


There are lies, damn lies and statistics.... Yes Intel and ATI have a higher market share than nVidia however you are comparing different things. Intel only supplies integrated graphics that go into either budget desktops or highly portable laptops. Most of those machines tend to end up being used in the business setting and mostly on Windows as we all know who is dominating the business desktop. They also end up being in Intel servers as they don't exactly need graphics power (or graphics in general). You are also forgetting that even machines that have integrated video can run discrete video adapters so just because Intel shipped 4million integrated units doesn't mean that 2 million of them don't end up running nVidia. It's the same with ATI, traditionally ATI provides alot of integrated solutions as well as mobile video. nVidia has always been more prominent in standalone desktop graphics solutions. Also you need to consider that because of the support that is provided by the binary driver a very large number of Linux machines uses nVidia solutions for their graphics needs. Even though worldwide nVidia accounts for about 20% of the market (innacurate figure for all the reasons above) in Linux machines it may very well account for 80% of the market.

In fact, since we are the ones who like Ubuntu how it is right now, and it's the driver-by-default people who want change, it's even more appropriate for them to go find another distro instead of us.

But, despite that, I feel it's rude and non-productive to tell other Ubuntu users to like it or lump it (which is what friendly suggestions to find another distro are really saying). We all have a right to say what direction we feel the distro we use should take.

---
