### Post by BuffaloX on 2007-01-06
> **prizrak said:**
> 
BTW who's Mike? :p 


Probably Mark..:-k 

And prizrak you are absolutely right about Intel, they are NOTHING on the graphics market. Their graphic chips are not even considered to be 3D by most of the market.

About drivers and firmware:
There's a HUGE difference between firmware and drivers, and there's a HUGE difference between kernel space and user space.

Traditionally firmware existed only in ROMs, today some firmware exist partly in RAM as part of the driver, but that's only to make systems cheaper and more flexible.

Linus and most kernel developers has no problem with firmware being closed, but the driver must be open. This is because firmware does not directly influence the kernel. Drivers on the other hand integrates with kernel, if the driver is closed, you cannot upgrade your kernel without great risk of malfunction of the closed source driver.
This was what happened with the great disaster on Dapper 6.06. which reflected very badly on Ubuntu, and Linux in general.

To understand the difference between firmware and driver better, you might think of a printer. The driver accepts a system char code, which it translates to the proper printer code. The firmware translates the printer code into the proper low level commands needed to print it.
If you upgrade kernel to enable unicode instead of ASCII, the driver must be upgraded to accept unicode , and then send the proper printer codes, The printer "firmware" doesn't know the diffrence, but your system has been succesfully upgraded.

Sometimes distinction between driver and firmware can be unclear, but basically if it works as part of the firmware it's OK. It doesn't matter if it's on ROM or in RAM loaded by the driver.

The average user however knows nothing about all this, but get pissed at Linux, claiming it's unstable, because the system breaks, like the infamous Dapper X breakage.

Proprietary drivers limits choice of kernel, and thus limits the supported hardware base.
This is part of the claim that Linux doesn't support enough hardware, distributions hold back on kernel upgrades, because it breaks systems with Nvidea/ATI or other proprietary drivers. The newest hardware supported in new kernels don't get distributed. So closed source drivers are already harming Linux.

The difference between user space and kernel space, is that the kernel license doesn't apply to user space. In user space you can do whatever you want, and never think of the kernel license. You can make all the closed source Linux software you want.

---

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-07
> **prizrak said:**
> Really? According to this poll there are 30% of users who want them by default and 15% that don't. The rest want choice at install time, which does not rule out them wanting those drivers. For all you know they could want the drivers but also respect other's views and the right to choose (like me for instance). This is the issue with statistics they are completely useless.

They are useless if you don't apprehend the definition of terms such as "by default" and "automatic". "By default" and "automatic" both mean that *there is no "choice"*: the decision is made for the user, no option is proffered prior to installation to disallow the activation of the proprietary drivers. Someone responding that there should be a "choice" indeed does rule out them wanting those drivers being **AUTOMATICALLY INSTALLED** -- which is what Zetetic initially stated (the exact words being, "70% of Ubuntu users don't want binary drivers **by default**").

Of course we need to exercise caution when taking polls and interpreting the results; if you wish to argue that wording of this poll was misleading -- that the respondants might not know the definition of "automatically" and more explanation is needed -- then you should discuss that. If you think that the poll is improperly weighted towards representing the more "advanced" Ubuntu users (by virtue of it being held in the Ubuntu forums), you might very well present *that*.

What you should NOT be doing is misquoting (or incompletely quoting) the statements of others and then refuting some fictitious statement that was never made.

### Post by BuffaloX on 2007-01-07
> **saulgoode said:**
> If you wish to argue that wording of this poll was misleading -- that the respondants might not know the definition of "automatically"

I think you are over interpreting, you can have different levels of automatic, including an option to enable or disable it. I think that's what's meant by "choice" in the third option.
It can even default to automatic, and still give you an option to select another setting than the default. Default only means that it is the setting used, unless it's changed.
Automatic is only automatic if it's enabled.

Wanting an option to select or deselect something, doesn't in itself imply which you prefer.
You can argue about circomstancial implications, but in that case I rather think it's like this.

Today there is no option to select automatic, and automatic isn't default.
Wanting an option to choose automatic therefore implies that you want to use it.

### Post by Dr. C on 2007-01-07
> **BuffaloX said:**
> Exactly, some sort of solution with Nvidea and ATI, that solves this would be nice.
They are the two major thorns in the eye right now.

Until then I think the best solution is to give people the option to do an informed choice.
But Proprietary drivers are still a violation of the GPL license, I hope Mark and the Ubuntu team can come up with some sort of compromise that doesn't piss too many off.

As you said earlier, most users just want the system to work. and I think that's true.
Wobbly windows and the works. :D

IANAL. Proprietary drivers are not in themselves a violation of the GPL license. There is nothing in the GPL to prevent a **user** from incorporating propriety code or binaries into GPL software, creating a dervied work and **using** the derived work. The GPL violation, and consequential software piracy, occurs only if the derived work is **distributed**. That why it is critical that the "derived work" only be created by the end user after the end user has made an informed decision, and not by a distributer. This is not just about "pissing people off", there are important legal issues here that need to be carefully addressed. 

I am not opposed to propriety drivers, infact I use the NVIDA drivers on my Dapper installation, but I am very much opposed to the distribution of a derived work involving both GPL code and propriety code, since that latter is just a form of software piracy.

---

Here is my question: Is a Linux kernel with binary blobs a derived work under the GPL? 

If the answer is yes, which I suspect to be the case (I stand to be corrected on this) then this derived work can only be created by an end user and never by a distributer in order to be in complicance with the GPL.

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-08
> **FineE said:**
> Here is my question: Is a Linux kernel with binary blobs a derived work under the GPL? 

If the answer is yes, which I suspect to be the case (I stand to be corrected on this) then this derived work can only be created by an end user and never by a distributer in order to be in complicance with the GPL.

My own feeling (though I claim no particular legal expertise) is that your suspicion is correct (i.e., the answer is "yes"). 

Krickenstein's interpretation of Linus's comments is quite close to my own, however, I would provide a slightly different characterization of the part about the law being used to enforce ideology. My interpretation was that Linus was saying that copyright holders should not be enforcing copyright law (if that is not what he was saying then I will express it as my own opinion :) ). 

Just because the kernel developers are the owners of the copyright does not mean that they should take specific measures to prevent violations of the copyright (this would be similar to the DRM approach taken by the media industry). Nonetheless, that does not mean that they should not take action (through the courts) if their copyrights are violated.

It is the responsibility of a distribution to comply with the copyright license of the software it provides -- just as a book publisher must honor the licenses of its products. There can be no legal remedy before a violation occurs (in the more commercial world, injunctions might be served; but it is unlikely the FSF would bother with that as the concern here is not over loss of revenue).

My own assessment of the three options presented in this poll from the standpoint of GPL compliance is as follows (I will use NVidia as the example of a proprietary kernel driver):

OPTION #1: Would be an obvious violation because Linux+NVidia is a derived work created by the distribution. It does not matter if the linking is done at installation if it is performed without input from the user; the distribution makes the decision that bundles the two entities and there can be no argument that the user performed the linking (remember, this is *my* opinion). Consider if it were NVidia who was distributing the product, bundling Linux with their drivers and offering it to customers as a demonstration of their products' capabilities. Should not those who created Linux be able to say that NVidia can't use their "intellectual property" in this manner? If you would agree that NVidia should not be permitted to do this then by what logic are you justifying Ubuntu should be?

OPTION #2: Definitely in compliance with the GPL. Keeping the drivers separate respects both the wishes and rights of the kernel copyright holders. The user is permitted by the licensing to make whatever modifications to his system he wishes (Section 0 of the GPL) and this includes his right to modify his kernel by installing proprietary drivers.

OPTION #3: Here we have the "gray area" which is so problematic to this debate. The user is provided the choice to have the NVidia drivers linked to the kernel during installation, presumably by a simple "yes/no" dialog at installation. Would the courts see this as the user being the one who is actually installing the software (thereby complying with the GPL)? Or would they view it as contrived measure to circumvent the intent of the GPL?  To be honest, I am not sure. My own view is that offering the user a choice would be a nice resolution to this debate, but my suspicion is that courts would still view it as infringement. Again, consider if it were NVidia providing the distribution and telling you "Click on this button because we can't legally do it for you". Would this scenario seem reasonably compliant to a copyright judge?

---

### Post by Tomosaur on 2007-01-08
I agree with Option 3 described above. There is no real necessity to mention anything specific in the optional driver installation dialog. All that would need to be asked is 'do you want to enable 3d acceleration' (for a video driver, for example), then you could choose your graphics card from a list, if polling (ie, the software probes the device to get a product id) is not possible, or type the name in and the software goes off and attempts to find the correct driver via the net. There is absolutely no need to provide the drivers on the install disk, and I'm confident that the method I just described is perfectly legal.

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-08
> **prizrak said:**
> The nVidia driver is a userspace driver not a kernel driver. It is an X module that is loaded through a GPL'ed shim that is part of the kernel. I would assume FGRLX to be the same thing so there is no GPL violation either way. Well maybe there is, have to ask the X devs about it since it's up to them to allow/disallow such things.
You may be correct in your assessment but my opinion differs. In order for the shim you describe to be GPLed, it must comply with the GPL. It is not sufficient to declare software to be under the GPL and then distribute in a non-compliant manner (Section 7 of the GPL states, "If you cannot distribute so as to satisfy simultaneously your obligations under this License and any other pertinent obligations, then as a consequence you may not distribute the Program at all").

If the sole purpose of the shim is to be linked to a non-GPL driver then it would seem to me that it is not complying (i.e., the shim+NVidia is a derived work of the two entities) and therefore whether or not a shim is employed does not change the overall issue. If the case were presented that the shim does more than just provide linkage between a proprietary driver and the kernel then the situation is not so clear. The NDISwrapper plug-in, for example, theoretically works with some GPL-compliant drivers as well as with closed, proprietary ones intended for Windows. Distribution of the NDISwrapper might be considered compliant because of this distinction. 

Does the NVidia shim work with GPLed video drivers? I don't know the answer to this. If it does then your perspective would have more merit to me. If it does not then I don't feel that the shim alters the situation.

This basically satisfies the letter of the GPL if not the spirit of it. One by product of such design is the nVidia driver not taking out your entire system when it crashes since it's just a userland application.

As far as being able to use it for a different driver, I'm sure it can be rewritten but it's not necessary. The GPL'ed\Open Sourced drivers can be compiled as kernel modules and run in kernel space (better way of doing it anyway).

### Post by ZylGadis on 2007-01-08
It does not really matter what the shim is or does. Here is the case:

(GPL code) is linked to (non-GPL code that does not enforce everything GPL does) is linked to (proprietary code).

Both immediate relationships are legal. The transitive one, however, is not.

There are clauses in the GPL that explicitly state the GPL-enforced restrictions are transitive across such links. Everything else - userland, kernels, somebody's opinions or willingness to sue - is irrelevant. The kernel developers don't really oppose nvidia's driver because it does not (usually) break their thing. That has absolutely nothing to do with the legal picture, which is as stated above.

Addition: What you are concentrating on is just one of the most important clauses of the GPL, which also exists in the LGPL: derived works should be covered under the same license. The GPL's other most important clause, which LGPL lacks, is that any code linked to GPL code must be Free. That is why corporations are generally fine with LGPL code (e.g. JBOSS) but loathe the GPL, and that is why Ballmer called the GPL a virus. It does not make any difference whatsoever if the nvidia driver is considered a derived work or not as long as it is linked to GPL code.

Of course, you may argue about the definition of "linked." But, barring that, the legal picture is clear.

---

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-08
> **prizrak said:**
>  If the said driver is on the disk and is installed at install time (especially after agreement by the user) but not actually compiled into the distribution then it would also not be covered by the GPL.

I recognize your point and would concede that the technicalities of the GPL might be honored by such an approach. From a practical standpoint, I think it is fair to characterize copyright courts as being generally unsympathetic towards those who would exploit licensing "loopholes" in an effort to circumvent the rights of copyright holders. 

It is my thinking that any judment on this issue would hinge mostly on how much the installation was an action of the user (permitted by the GPL) and how much it was the action of the distribution. This is why I consider the automatic installation (with no user involvement) to be a violation. I also think that a separate program which was executed post-install by the end user would be in compliance. 

Between those two extremes it is indeed uncertain but I suspect that a copyright judge would not consider the end-user merely opting whether an installation should or should not take place to be sufficient to constitute a "user installation" (I am not certain of this, by any means).

This is all, of course, just my characterization of the situation. I am trying to be objective in presenting it though I admit that my allegiance lie completely with the authors of GPL software and I wish to respect their wishes in how their software is to be used despite any technical legalities or personal inconvenience.

### Post by ZylGadis on 2007-01-08
Prizrak, please read carefully and don't skip my points. It is not a question of derivative code (this is an entirely separate issue that may or may not be true - I personally don't think a driver is a derivative of a kernel in any way). If the kernel was LGPL, the discussion of derivatives would be necessary and relevant. As the kernel is GPL, that discussion should be a separate, side, and minor issue.

Regardless of the question of derivatives, my point from my previous post stands. Any code linked to GPL code should be Free. Transitive relationships are covered. I see nothing unclear about the nvidia binary driver - distributing it with the kernel is illegal unless someone manages to refute that it is linked to the kernel (or to something that is linked to the kernel).

I don't see how Torvalds's position makes the most sense, either. Perhaps it makes the most sense for him, as he has repeatedly stated that he is "just an engineer." I consider his position to be:

1. inconsiderate of the community he himself helped build
2. endorsing illegal practices
3. devoid of farsight and long-term thinking.

Practicality is not everything. If it were, the OS we use would be called BSD/Linux, not GNU/Linux, and would be proprietary. There already exists a similar one: it is called OSX.

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-09
> **ZylGadis said:**
> Prizrak, please read carefully and don't skip my points. It is not a question of derivative code (this is an entirely separate issue that may or may not be true - I personally don't think a driver is a derivative of a kernel in any way). If the kernel was LGPL, the discussion of derivatives would be necessary and relevant. As the kernel is GPL, that discussion should be a separate, side, and minor issue.

ZylGadis, whether or not derivation and linking are separate issues, it is appropriate to expect compliance with *both* these aspects of the GPL. This also may just be a case of our mislabelling things or sharing differing interpretations of "derivation". There can be overlapping between the two issues and we may be confusing each other by not distinguishing whether we are addressing a specific case or making a more general assessment.

When you mention that you "don't think a driver is a derivative of a kernel in any way", are you applying that to kernel *modules*, or just to userland device drivers? Because to me it seems very clear that kernel modules *are* derived from the kernel. This is why I approached the NVidia argument from the standpoint of it being the "shim" that was out of compliance, not the NVidia driver. The shim runs in kernel address space and intimately accesses kernel services. I believe this to be true for any kernel module. Perhaps you could clarify this point.

I am also slightly confused by your argument that "any code linked to GPL code should be Free". It is not that I disagree with your statement, just that I think it unlikely for the FSF to use it as an argument with regards to the Linux kernel. I suspect that you would agree that the FSF is unhappy with the kernel developers' unwillingness to draw a clear line between what parts of the kernel can be linked to by non-Free software and whichmay not. Linus seems to be satisfied with leaving that a "gray" area; while the FSF wishes it to be more "black and white", they don't necessarily want it to be "all black" and it would be unlikely that they would endorse your contention that the kernel is pure GPL. 

I agree with your position on Linus' attitude; even to the point of it encouraging illegal practices. His desire to keep everything "gray" is akin to someone not wanting to draw property lines, instead opting to wait for someone to stray and then accusing them of trespassing. I understand the significance of the GPL_ONLY tag on symbols, but in my opinion a far better solution would be to have specific interfaces of the kernel released under the LGPL. 

I hope you understand the gist of what I am saying, I realize that I did not describe it very well but perhaps you could address this in our own words or from your own perspective. I think we share a common understanding of the problem and I should like to know your thoughts.

---

### Post by prizrak on 2007-01-09
> I am also slightly confused by your argument that "any code linked to GPL code should be Free". It is not that I disagree with your statement, just that I think it unlikely for the FSF to use it as an argument with regards to the Linux kernel. I suspect that you would agree that the FSF is unhappy with the kernel developers' unwillingness to draw a clear line between what parts of the kernel can be linked to by non-Free software and whichmay not. Linus seems to be satisfied with leaving that a "gray" area; while the FSF wishes it to be more "black and white", they don't necessarily want it to be "all black" and it would be unlikely that they would endorse your contention that the kernel is pure GPL.
Even more so, different people us the word "linking" differently. If linking is making system calls then there is no requirement in the GPL to make the code that is doing it GPL. That in turn depends on whether you could consider system calls an "output" (the GPL explicitly states that output doesn't have to be GPL'ed). If linking is using specific libraries that access certain function then it can be considered a derivative. It would also be important what license the libraries are under. This is very gray legally speaking and is very difficult to guard against.

---

### Post by dv_ on 2007-01-09
Binary drivers?
If you just do some office stuff or other things not involving anything beyond very simple 3D graphics, you can choose.

However, for actual state of the art 3D interactive graphics programming involving state of the art effects, there is no choice: you have to use the binary drivers.

OpenGL is praised as the good and cool 3D standard, while Direct3D is despised as being MS-centric (which is right, however D3D is a nice API regardless...!) Now, if nvidia and ati get alienated from the Linux community, two things will happen:

a) Linux will lose any chance of being adopted as a gaming platform. The chance is extremely slim already, this step will cement it at 0%. Also, forget about any projects like Gelato or CoreImage, which use the GPU for accelerating computations.
b) *Interesting* OpenGL hacking will be possible in OSX, FreeBSD, Solaris, Windows, but not in Linux.

Point (b) is ironic; the free 3D standard would not be available on the free OS, but on propietary and BSD-systems only.

In fact, it seems that the Linux crowd likes being ignorant about graphics programming. "Its just games stuff" seems to be the generall attitude. Fact is that many aspects of graphics programming are *extremely* hard to grasp and implement (especially 3D graphics). Graphics coders are not just some kiddies writing toy software while the adults write their leet network code. I'm willing to bet most self-proclaimed Linux hardcore hackers would fail at implementing a decent 3D engine.

---

### Post by Miguel on 2007-01-18
Actually, the command you should use is
```

netstat -tap

```

You are only interested in the ones marked as LISTENING. Please remember that if you are browsing the net, your firefox will be listed there. Another way to do it is using nmap (not installed by default) to see what ports you have open:
```

nmap your.ip.address

```
Here, you shouldn't use localhost or 127.0.0.1 as your IP, because some services are listening in localhost, but not any other address. As a final note, ubuntu doesn't have *any* port open after a default install (a behaviour actually critisized by Avahi developers). You will have open ports if you install apache or openssh-server or those things. Debian has more open ports by default.

---

elachistos| nmap -P0 xx.ccc.vvv.bbb

### Post by Kernel Sanders on 2007-01-21
Surely the goal here is to have a free, fully featured, open source distro, that is close to perfect right out of the box? (As in, it needs no additional tweaking to get things to work)

Therefore, as far as the current NVidia/ATI open source drivers go, Ubuntu is failing. For example: I have had enormous problems with Edgy because of my ATI Radeon graphics card, and I lack the knowledge/ability to fix this at my current stage of Linux n00byness :(

Therefore, at the moment, these drivers are very much needed. Both for n00bs like myself to have the system "just work" out of the box, and for Ubuntu to continue to develop its multimedia capabilities upon a solid platform?

To use the old adage, "you work with what you have, not what you wish you had"

We all wish we had open source drivers that did what we needed, but we dont, we have propriatory ones.

My opinion :)

### Post by darkhatter on 2007-01-21

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-21
> ***John* said:**
> So because you cant achieve that now, its fine to sacrifice out of the box usability? Its fantastic to have an ideal, but you must employ a certain degree of pragmatism along the way.

Then by all means, let's be pragmatic. Can you provide a single example of incorporating proprietary software by default in a distribution having led to the eventual open sourcing of that software? Off the top of my head, I can think of several examples to the contrary: Java, Netscape/Mozilla, RPC, MySQL, OOo -- not to mention the vast number of projects that chose the OS model from the outset based on the market place benefits Free Software offers.

Perhaps after you finish answering that simple question, you could provide some sort of estimate of how many new users this move is anticipated to attract? The pragmatic businessman would certainly not embark on such a controversial endeavor without having some concept in mind of the expected result. So what is it? 5%? 10%? 20%? 90%? 

Taking the response to that query, is the expected conversion to Linux going to be enough to convince hardware manufacturers to encourage OS drivers? Exactly what percentage is expected to satisfy these vendors that supporting OSed drivers is worthwhile? Pragmatism dictates that you have some idea of this factor and that you base it on some sort of precedent, formulation, or assurance from the manufacturer. 

Please, let me know what the "pragmatic" reasoning is behind this decision; because thus far it sounds like a bunch of ad hoc rationalization for eschewing the benefits of Free Software without any real justification for the expectation of recompense.

---

---

### Post by saulgoode on 2007-01-22
> **prizrak said:**
> I think you are missing the point, it's not that nVidia/AMD will all of a sudden open up their drivers because Ubuntu wants to include it. It's that while work is being done on open drivers (such as nouveau) there is an intermediate solution.

No, I am not missing the point. In fact, I agree with your point that this is about making things work *now*. It is not about increasing marketshare, it is not about applying pressure on the hardware vendors, and it is not even a short-term compromise to achieve long-term goals. In short, this proposal in no way supports the Ubuntu philosophy; this is not a move that in the long haul supports it, and in the short-term, by the very nature of the move, it directly contradicts the expressed Ubuntu philosophy.

Now even though I agree with you that the real issue is the "intermediate solution" aspect of the argument, I do not agree that the proposal is the best solution (or even a good one). I firmly believe that adopting this intermediate solution would have the net effect of prolonging the interim period before a "final" solution is reached and I suspect it would have the more immediate effect of making the distribution *less* useful. 

Binary drivers are not the panacea that you seem to assume them to be. Aspects such as programming, documentation, testing, debugging, and maintenance all become more difficult for Linux developers (kernel, distribution, and application). Vendors have little incentive for maintaining support for older hardware, supporting "non-standard" Linux systems, or providing updates to fix bugs or support newer programming needs. 

Issues such as philosophy and legality aside, I personally do not think that blackbox hardware and proprietary drivers are a viable solution and I would predict that attempts to incorporate them into the Ubuntu distribution will lead to problems much worse and more far-reaching than just "educating" the user about Free Software. You might very well disagree with me on this and I respect that. I recognize the "just make it work" argument much more readily than I do the "the ends justify the means" one.

---

### Post by starscalling on 2007-02-14
my views on this currently 84 page threaded topic:

1. it is a GOOD THING that ubuntu wants to work as best as 'humanly' possible out of the box

2. it is a BAD THING to encourage hardware manufacturers to continue with closed source drivers.

i mean really i dont give a damn im installying w32codecs or mplayer compiled just for me.. but i legally own several versions of windows not all of which are installed :P so i could argue the point that i have the right to do so. i do use the nvidia driver b/c i need its support for a few things.. the free drivers just dont work. but on the other hand ppl like me tired of things like windows and how things are done there contribute to projects that make drivers that are open source for us. i mean how long must we put up with this attitude that even though the end user purchased something they dont really own it. i mean for chr1$$@k35 its in my HANDS, i PAYED for it, your not gonna replace it if it breaks after a certain point.. i mean I OWN THIS; so deal with it if i wanna make it work in ways you didnt dream of at the time or didnt really want. 

after all
isnt that the whole point of linux?
an os designed for hackers by hackers?
its nice we have ubuntu 
dont get me wrong i love it.. ive converted a few dozen ppl to at least dual boot systems [btw the reason i still have windows is b/c i use it to hack my cell phone =/] i'm not asking for a miricle but lets face it most ppl dont care... but one thing i have learned over the years is that there is always a cost for anything. and one must look closer for it when something is free. in linux's case for me the cost was learning how to do things in (a) new way(s).
linux for me is like a hobby; but i dont see support for things like point or encompass- things that are used quite hardcore in the business world. exchange server is another point there... and yes i know there are a few free apps and here we are supposed to be talking about drivers.

now on the poll i voted that it should be avaliable BY USER CHOICE. because from what i was led to believe by the wonderful guy'z n gal'z that introduced me to a working linux desktop was that it was about choice. 

perhaps they should be offered during install... lots of drivers should be offered... but we should consider if this is an online or offline install. one wonderful thing that debian has is that netinstall cd. if one is installing from a method like that then the drivers should be downloaded from repos. but if its a fully offline installation then perhaps the drivers should be shipped with the cd[s]. consider also the impact on offered applications by taking that space up with drivers. again, one must give to get.

perhaps its time we sought out multi cd's - perhaps a second cd-rom with proprietary stuff on it? perhaps its time to migrate towards a dvd distro [not that the dvd needs be full but it could at least hold more stuff - say a gig?]

in the end i'm counting on hackers to protect me from drm
from bill gates and his compadre's

and dont get me started on software patents... i just DONT wanna go there.
btw can i suggest that tor / vidalia be shipped by default? :>

so the consequenses of shipping these proprietary drivers:
its not that ppl will use them. its that ppl will not trust the open - sourced drivers when they are at least at a beta stage... i mean the nv works for single monitor systems quite well out of the box, the install directions are rather easy, one must get the cdrom or dvdrom from somewhere so it is either purchased or downloaded... its not like its absolutely needed to include in there for one to look at the screen....
but what about when the end user is presented with say nv nvidia and drivernx. nv is open source, nvidia is closed as usual and third is open again. from experienceing nv / nvidia they might just conclude without actually trying it that the drivernx is no good for what they want.
and what do they want you ask?
they want everything working~! they want it pretty, and they want it easy to do! [which if you look at vista can be a rather tall order as in my opinion vista is still 2 years prior to desktop ready].

personally i get 'bored' easily and i enjoy solving challenging problems, i however, am not the norm. i will try the new drivers... but will u?

---
